Exchange operators cut dependence on legacy trading business

Philip Stafford in London 
640 words
21 September 2019
11:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Rival bids offer two visions for LSE as insatiable demand provides bourses with promise for future

Mergers and acquisitions among exchanges has sometimes been likened to a game of chess. In a world where prized assets are scarce, a move by a major player forces the hand of another.

So it is withHong Kong Exchanges and Clearing, which is taking a rebuffed 32bn bid for theLondon Stock Exchange Group to shareholders. The Chinese exchange is hoping to break up a $27bn deal the LSE struck to buyRefinitiv, a data and trading group.

If either deal completes it will be by far the largest exchanges deal in history, and underscore how the industry s biggest bourses have been radically remoulded since the turn of the century.

 It has been such a dynamic industry; driven by regulatory, geopolitical, macroeconomic, technological changes,  said David Schwimmer, chief executive of the LSE, said on Thursday.

In the late 1990s exchanges began facing competition as electronic competition began to eat at their profits. The broad response was the shed their mutual status and hunt for new markets.

A period of intense dealmaking began, characterised by megadeals and dramatic interventions. In the mid-noughties the CME Group set itself up to become the world s largest exchange by market value with the $8bn purchase of the Chicago Board of Trade and an $8.9bn deal for the New York Mercantile Exchange. Intercontinental Exchange, which also bid for the CBOT, consoled itself with the New York Board of Trade for $1bn.

The LSE bought Borsa Italiana and Nasdaq and Deutsche Brse   having both failed with bids for the LSE   purchased OMX and the International Securities Exchange. The New York Stock Exchange used its shares to acquire Archipelago and merge with Euronext.

That heightened buying spree slowed as the crisis led regulators and politicians to reassess their assumptions about financial markets and flex their muscles. A turning point was the Australian government blocking Singapore s SGX from buying ASX, operator of the main Australian exchange, in 2011.

Deutsche Brse suffered twice when EU antitrust regulators blocked its mergers with the LSE and NYSE Euronext. Successful big deals were rare. ICE   a commodity derivatives bourse which was only founded in 1999   grabbed attention with the $8.2bn purchase of the NYSE in 2013.

Those that were approved tended to be where there was little competitive overlap. Exchanges had begun to realise that ambitious cross-border deals were fraught with manybeginning to see themselves very differently to their histories as venues for trading. It was the world s insatiable demand for data that offered the most promise for the future.

ICE made the first pivot, purchasing Interactive Data Corp for $5.2bn. Others soon followed; Deutsche Brse bought Axioma, an analytics company, in a deal worth $850m.

LSE shareholders now can choose between two different deals that will define its future. Mr Schwimmer has put his bet on Refinitiv, arguing there are two big drivers of change.

 One is the increasing importance of data and analytics. The other is with more and more electronic trading, we think there will be an increasing importance of multi-asset class trading and investment strategies. 

HKEX is offering a more traditional exchange deal based on connecting China s markets with the more than $1.8tn of derivatives business that flows through London every day.

 The combination of HKEX and the LSE makes perfect sense from a strategic perspective 	. 	. 	. 	but the reality is that there s little chance this transaction will gain the regulatory and political approvals necessary to consummate it,  said David Tabaka, analyst at Burton-Taylor.

Shareholders will not only have to weigh up their vision of the future, but which deal has the best chance of success.

'I like to do things that are beautiful'

Stephen Schwarzman 
2,514 words
21 September 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
3
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Lunch with the FT

There are only two rules at Blackstone: no internal politics and do not lose money. Over poached lobster in Mayfair, the private equity firm's combative co-founder talks to Lionel Barber about risk-taking, Trump whispering and why the rich shouldn't have to pay more tax

W hen Stephen Schwarzman originally proposed lunch, he suggested two venues: Claridge's or St Tropez. Naively, I assumed there was a choice. A rendezvous on the Cte d'Azur, where Schwarzman enjoys one of his many palatial residences, was always a tad risky for a man who loves to control the situation.

Invited for 12.15pm, I spot Schwarzman standing centre stage in The Foyer & Reading Room restaurant. Short and tanned, he's wearing a lightly pinstriped dark suit with his favourite combination: blue-striped shirt with white collar and purple tie. He's put on a few pounds since he was a top runner in high school and college, but at 72 he still looks trim.

Encounters with Schwarzman are rarely dull. He's a combative character who, while outwardly charming, does not take kindly to criticism. We've exchanged expletives in Davos and in his luxuriously appointed office at Blackstone, the Wall Street private equity and asset management firm he co-founded 34 years ago. But there have never been any hard feelings.

Schwarzman's fortune (net worth about $18bn) has bought him power and influence. He's graduated from being a mega dealmaker to philanthropist, back channel in US-China relations and "Trump whisperer". I want to explore these multiple roles, but also pin down why the man who has built one of the most successful financial businesses on the planet has never quite received the credit he believes he deserves.

A Wall Street rival offers a clue: "Steve is one of the most honest people I know. Most people lie to your face. He'll always tell you straight up he's going to screw you."

As we study our menus, I raise the character question. His restless, driven nature is legendary. "Yes, it's true," he admits. "It goes with the territory, the person." As if on cue, he announces we're moving table because of a cold draught, compounded by noisy neighbours.

Schwarzman says, half-joking, that unlike his younger twin brothers he inherited "the right gene mix" from his mother, "a very formidable, strong survivor". His father by contrast was "a lovely, super-bright but not ambitious person" who ran a linen store in Philadelphia. "We could have been Bed Bath & Beyond. I don't know why not. That's the way my mind has always worked, he just had no interest. Fascinating."

My guest is not given to self-doubt.

Accepted at Yale, he phoned the dean of admissions at Harvard to parley his way in. (He was rejected.) After Harvard Business School, he joined Lehman Brothers. He always coveted the top job, but the infighting was murderous and he ended up selling the failing bank to American Express. He struck out on his own, co-founding Blackstone in 1985 with Pete Peterson, the former Lehman chairman and Nixon-era commerce secretary. Our waiter arrives bearing water. Schwarzman orders tomato and basil soup, followed by poached lobster salad. I choose smoked salmon and grilled Dover sole off the bone. Alcohol is out because I am on dry August and my guest says he only drinks wine sparingly. (Schwarzman takes an anticoagulant pill a day to ward off phlebitis, a vein inflammation that killed his grandfather.) Blackstone started as a boutique advisory firm, with the goal of making enough money to start its own private equity fund. Private equity has attracted controversy because of alleged asset-stripping: buying companies, loading them with debt ("leverage") and selling them off at a handsome profit, with favourable tax treatment.

Schwarzman casts himself as a longterm investor, not a scavenger in sheep's clothing. He recounts with gusto the megadeals and the risk-taking involved in picking the right time to buy and sell assets, ranging from US Steel's railroad network to the Waldorf hotel.

His skill is market timing. Blackstone has expanded into real estate and hedge funds and other "alternative assets", with $545bn under management today. Blackstone funds are also the largest owner of real estate in the world. The firm's rise epitomises the "buy side" revolution that favours asset managers at the expense of traditional banks trading liquid securities.

Schwarzman has written a book, which is part memoir, part Blackstone management primer called What It Takes: Lessons in the Pursuit of Excellence. (A better title would be Whatever It Takes, I suggest.) There are many valuable insights; perhaps the biggest is the list of endorsements featuring Henry Kissinger, Eric Schmidt, the ex-Google chairman, Janet Yellen, the ex-Fed chair, and Mark Carney, governor of the Bank of England.

Peterson and Schwarzman worked together for 33 years, but the father-son relationship fell apart at the end. Peterson felt he had been squeezed out of his fair share of an expanding business. Schwarzman argued that he was the rainmaker, the firm was hiring more people and something had to give. "He had reached a different stage in his career and he volunteered to reduce his interest in the firm and did."

This is the unsentimental side of Schwarzman. He appears not to care. He prides himself on his intuition (Blackstone went public ahead of the Great Crash in 2008 and, with fresh capital, positioned itself perfectly for the rebound). He also highlights his ability to recruit and retain high-quality executives such as Tony James, his longtime number two, and Jon Gray, the real estate guru, both fellow billionaires.

Blackstone is a meritocracy, he says, where two iron rules apply: no internal politics and do not lose money. "I go from the premise that anybody of talent does not want necessarily to be a private in an army. The lowest they want is to be a lieutenant colonel and preferably they'd all like to be generals."

Hang on, I say, there's no mistaking who's generalissimo at Blackstone. Schwarzman still signs all cheques above $250,000, retains a veto on major decisions and later declares: "I don't think I'll ever retire. My grandfather never did."

Anyway, surely someone loses money some time, I add.

He half-concedes the point: "Every once in a while there's some weird fucking thing where government does something or there's a technological breakthrough that's transformative that a reasonable person couldn't know what is going to happen. And so that introduces what I call unpredictable risk, and you can get, potentially, picked off by that."

I ask Schwarzman how his lobster tastes. "Pretty good," he replies, picking at the pink flesh. My Dover sole is fresh and tasty. So how does Schwarzman assess the state of modern capitalism? "Well, I would answer that with another question: what's the state of modern politics?" The "rejiggering" of governments in the west, he argues, is a legacy of the financial crisis, globalisation and a massive transfer of jobs and wealth to emerging markets such as China. And that's before the impact of technology. These trends have squeezed the middle class and lower-income groups.

His remedies start with an increase in the minimum wage to about $15, up from single digits. This will destroy some jobs, but it will directly benefit 15 to 20 per cent of the population, with a knock-on effect on compensation for another 20 per cent, he argues.

Schwarzman wants to dramatically improve education levels in the US. He would make teachers tax exempt so as to raise wages and create "a new privileged class". Two-thirds of America's workforce went to high school or less, he says. "This is not the workforce of the future."

Finally, he would encourage the Baby Boom generation that's retiring to take up jobs as mentors and assistant teachers. (In a pre-lunch gesture, he offered to donate all book proceeds to Lucy Kellaway's like-minded charity, Now Teach. The offer was accepted.) How about the mega rich paying more taxes? Schwarzman looks incredulous. "Taxes on the wealthy?" Well, why not? "The tax system in the US is extremely progressive ... it's a reverse pyramid. The top 10 per cent pays 70 per cent [of the tax take]. The top 1 per cent pays 38 to 40. I pay around 48 to 47 per cent tax rate." (He later clarifies this is a "blended rate" covering ordinary income, capital gains and dividends.) Schwarzman can be tin-eared. He once compared a proposal to tax private equity executives selling their publicly listed stock as ordinary income to Hitler invading Poland. He says it was a private conversation, but does not deny railing against the "semi-socialist" Obama administration. Today, socialism is no longer a dirty word in the Democratic party. Is he worried about the 2020 presidential election? "The politics of anger and free things is a powerful potential elector," he says, adding that the US is more polarised than ever in his lifetime. "Vietnam was about one issue. This is much more intense, much more far-reaching."

The gravity of the conversation demands light relief, happily provided by the waiter, who congratulates Schwarzman on his choice of the upside down strawberry tart as dessert.

Waiter: "It's the new style."

LB: "New style?" Waiter: "Yes sir." LB: "What's the old style? Waiter: "A regular strawberry tart." Schwarzman won't talk on the record about Donald Trump, a wearily familiar response from chief executives, even billionaires. Yet as chairman and chief executive of Blackstone, he's running the firm while also advising Trump. He says the potential conflicts of interest are manageable, but there have been awkward moments, especially after he had to disband the chief executive panel advising Trump in the wake of far-right violence in Charlottesville in 2017.

Did Trump equivocate about the farright? "Most of us had no idea there were Nazis in America. That may mean we're naive ... We also had no idea there was something called anti-fascistas who also used violence. The whole thing was completely disorienting."

Trump, he concludes, did not get his words right first time. The second time, it "looked right". By the third time, the US had experienced "a societygalvanising moment".

S chwarzman has been accused of wearing too many hats. In China, where Blackstone has important business, he's tried to be a bridge builder; but he agrees that relations between Beijing and the US are at a low point.

"China has enjoyed the most stunning transformation in the past 40 years of any country, most probably, in world history. It did it through a variety of mechanisms that are not consistent with the rules and regulations of the developed world."

Now, in effect, the US is asking Beijing to adopt western practices. This, he admits, is very difficult. But, he suggests, the Chinese leadership has been told that however difficult they think Trump is, the Democrats could be far worse in terms of trade protectionism.

We turn to Schwarzman's generous philanthropy. In recent years, he has donated $100m to the New York Public Library, $150m to Yale, 150m to Oxford university, $350m to MIT. He's also set up the Schwarzman scholars, a one-year masters programme on global affairs for top international students to study at Tsinghua University in Beijing. (Full disclosure: I have served on the 200-strong panel of judges for the past four years.) Schwarzman says he only wants to donate to causes that are "paradigm shifting", such as the artificial intelligence lab at MIT. But he is a hard-boiled negotiator when it comes to disbursing money and naming rights. The New York Public Library became so frustrated that they proposed putting his name on the roof (that way his name would be seen by every plane flying over Manhattan to La Guardia). The funds came through. (Schwarzman's name is now on every entrance to its main building.) The ego has landed in many other prestigious locations. His 60th birthday party in 2007, featuring Rod Stewart in Manhattan's Armory, was reported to have closed Park Avenue ("It was only one lane," he protests). His 70th was an equally extravagant affair with an ancient Silk Road theme held at a fivestar hotel near President Trump's Mara-Lago estate in Florida. Why is glitz and showiness so integral to Steve Schwarzman? "That's a good question," he says, attributing his aesthetic taste partly to his second wife, Christine. "I like generally doing things that nobody has done. I also like to do things that are beautiful."

He points to the striking glass sculpture in the Foyer, with writhing snakes hanging like a chandelier. This was part of the refurbishment of Claridge's, a trophy picked up when Blackstone bought the Savoy Group 21 years ago. "I actually went out to the state of Washington and met with the sculptor Dale Chihuly to check it out."

Schwarzman wonders aloud how lucky he has been a rare moment when the scrappy self-made man lets himself go. I note that we are meeting on the 100th anniversary of the death of Andrew Carnegie, the Scottishborn steel magnate, one of the world's greatest philanthropists who gave away his entire fortune (about $300bn at today's prices).

"Do you agree with Carnegie when he said: he who dies rich dies disgraced?" Schwarzman offers a rambling answer about America's "tax infrastructure". So will he follow Warren Buffett's rallying call to the rich to donate more than 50 per cent of their fortune to charity? (Buffett himself has gone higher, to 99 per cent). "Maybe I will, maybe I won't, but to me that's not relevant ... obviously I've given it a lot of thought and the vast amount will go to charity."

Two and a half hours have passed. I switch off the tape recorder. Schwarzman looks slightly relieved. Just as I'm preparing to settle the bill, Wall Street's arch dealmaker pulls out a copy of the FT and points to an ad highlighting the longlist of the McKinsey-FT business book of the year.

"What It Takes did not make the list. Is it really too late?" Whatever it takes.

Lionel Barber is editor of the FT THE FOYER & READING ROOM Claridge's, 49 Brook Street, Mayfair, London Roasted tomato and basil soup 15 Severn &Wye smoked salmon 28 Grilled Dover sole with French beans 48 Caesar salad with poached lobster 36 Hazelnut mystre 16 Upside-down strawberry tart 16 Total (including service and tip) 200

SoftBank reaps instant profit from Wirecard debt shuffle

Robert Smith and Arash Massoudi in London 
699 words
21 September 2019
04:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Japanese conglomerate had agreed a Buffett-like investment in German group

SoftBank cut its exposure to Wirecard this week within hours of signing a strategic tie-up with the German payments company, in a move that could net the Japanese conglomerate a big profit while taking little risk.

SoftBank announced in April that it had agreed to pour  ¬900m into Wirecard, providing a vital vote of confidence in the Aschheim-based company that has faced controversy over its accounting.

SoftBank did not purchase a stake in Wirecard, however. Instead it bought a convertib le bond, a type of debt that can be repaid in stock rather than cash.

Wirecard s shareholders gave final approval to the transaction on Wednesday, while also formally signing a  strategic cooperation agreement  with SoftBank. The following day, Credit Suisse sold a new  ¬900m Wirecard bond that is exchangeable for stock to a broad group of investors.

The new deal effectively means that SoftBank has taken the money it invested off the table, in what SoftBank executives described as a  return optimisation  measure.

SoftBank and Credit Suisse declined to comment. Wirecard did not respond to requests for comment.

Analysts at brokerage Stifel said that Thursday s deal offloads the  economic risk  of the Japanese group s bet on Wirecard, adding that the deal effectively allows SoftBank to make good on its April commitment  without having to provide funding .

SoftBank still has skin in the game, however, because Wirecard s shares have rallied strongly since it announced the deal. The Japanese group has retained exposure to 3m shares   now worth around  ¬450m   and can also profit on a further 3.9m shares up to a price of around  ¬230 per share. Wirecard s shares are currently trading at  ¬149.

The Wirecard investment was made through a special fund managed by SoftBank Investment Advisers, which manages the group s influential $100bn Vision Fund.

Capital for the deal came from SoftBank, some of its employees and Mubadala, Abu Dhabi s state investment company, according to people familiar with the matter. Mubadala is the second-biggest backer of the Vision Fund after Saudi Arabia s Public Investment Fund. Mubadala did not respond to requests for comment.

A senior SoftBank executive told the Financial Times that the Wirecard trade was inspired by Warren Buffett, who has a record of making highly structured investments in seemingly troubled companies that then benefit from his reputation.

Mr Buffett s Berkshire Hathaway bought $5bn of preferred equity in Goldman Sachs during the height of the financial crisis, for example. This paid a hefty 10 per cent annual dividend and netted Mr Buffett billions of dollars of profit when the investment bank later bought back his stake.

The so-called  repackaging  of SoftBank s convertible bond was sold to investors at less generous terms than those the Japanese group extracted from Wirecard.

While repackagings are relatively common for small deals, convertible bond investors and bankers said that it is unusual to see one conducted on such a large scale.

SoftBank frequently makes use of equity derivatives such as those seen on its Wirecard trade, often as a way of employing leverage   raising debt against its stakes that can be deployed on other investments. For example, a so-called   equity collar  on shares in Chinese internet giant Alibaba helped SoftBank raise the funds to buy shares in Arm, the UK chip designer, before a takeover bid in 2016.

SoftBank took profit on its Wirecard trade at a time when the group is coming under renewed scrutiny for its outsized bets on tech companies. Investors in SoftBank s Vision Fund are braced for hefty writedowns on some of its investments. One of them, WeWork, has shelved its public listing while valuations for freshly listed Uber and Slack have tumbled.

Wirecard said its cooperation agreement with SoftBank would allow both companies to  pursue global growth opportunities and synergy effects, including through the SoftBank ecosystem in the fields of digital financial services, data analysis and artificial intelligence .

*This article has been amended since original publication to clarify the potential profit SoftBank stands to make.

EXCHANGE --- Markets: Contract Expirations Push Stock Volatility Up

By Gunjan Banerji 
206 words
21 September 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B13
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Stock volatility jumped this week, with turbulence heightened on Friday as a flurry of derivatives expired.

The Cboe Volatility Index, or VIX, rose Friday, gaining about 9% as the S&P 500 ticked down to end the week. The options-based gauge traded at Cboe Global Markets Inc. tends to fall when the S&P 500 is rising, and vice versa.

Friday is so-called quadruple witching, which occurs four times a year and refers to the day that options and futures on both indexes and stocks expire.

Some investors and analysts say the simultaneous expiration can spur greater volatility in markets as volumes pick up. The day tends to boost trading activity because investors readjust expiring positions. Investors sometimes choose to roll over their positions so that they expire later in time or close them entirely.

Options give investors the right to buy or sell shares of stock at a specific price, later in time. Calls confer the right to buy, while puts give the right to sell.

"There's often increased volume and volatility," said Elisabeth Kashner, director of ETF research and analytics at FactSet.

WSJ Pro
VC Daily: Will VC Underperform?; Stripe Valuation Jumps; Datadog Debut Soars; Salesforce Backs Automattic

1,758 words
20 September 2019
14:02
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Happy Friday. Despite venture capital's current bull-market run, Josh Kopelman, co-founder and partner at First Round Capital, said he expects the asset class to underperform in the next few years.

Mr. Kopelman cited the increase in the number of growth funds, rising startup valuations and the faster speed with which deals get done that "are reducing the time to evaluate the opportunity," as reasons he believes venture returns overall would suffer.

Mr. Kopelman was speaking Wednesday at the Primary Venture Partners' summit in New York. He added that VCs need to have a thesis for why they believe they would outperform the asset class in this set of circumstances.

As of the first quarter of 2019, the Cambridge Associates LLC US Venture Capital Index, based on data on some 1,862 venture funds, has outperformed the S&P 500 Index over the past one, five, 15, 20 and 25 years, but it has underperformed if compared to the S&P 500 over the past three and 10-year intervals.

And now on to the news...

In the News

Mushroom deals. A startup backed by $80 million from investors is striking deals with a range of drug companies in its bid to lead the next wave of mental-health treatments, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/atai-life-sciences-is-assembling-a-mental-health-drug-platform-11568924265?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc&tesla=y".

* Munich-based Atai Life Sciences AG has formed and financed several companies creating new drugs from unusual and controversial substances including psilocybin, found in "magic mushrooms," and ketamine, which is nicknamed "Special K" and sometimes used illicitly.

* But the company intends to play a much larger part in a recent flurry of activity in drug-development innovation in mental health following decades of inactivity.

* Founded in 2017, Atai is riding a wave of renewed interest from researchers, investors and regulatory bodies in developing and commercializing new therapies from substances that might be controversial, overlooked or underused, such as the use of ketamine to treat depression, and MDMA, commonly known as ecstasy, to treat post-traumatic stress disorder.

Sky high. Stripe Inc. climbed closer to the top ranks of the highest-priced U.S. startups after a new funding round valued the financial-technology company at $35 billion, the Journal's Peter Rudegeair reports. Sequoia Capital, General Catalyst and Andreessen Horowitz were among the investors behind the $250 million investment, the company said Thursday. The $35 billion valuation, up about 50% from an early 2019 funding round, puts Stripe above Silicon Valley darlings Airbnb Inc. and Palantir Technologies Inc.

Blog cash. Automattic Inc., the parent company of web publishing and e-commerce platforms such as WordPress.com, has raised $300 million in its latest investment round, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/wordpress-parent-raises-300-million-11568934656?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc". Salesforce Ventures, the venture arm of Salesforce.com Inc., led the Series D round that values the San Francisco-based startup at $3 billion. That is three times its valuation after a $160 million Series C round in 2014, Automattic founder and Chief Executive Matt Mullenweg said. Mr. Mullenweg said in an interview that the capital will be used to scale up its products, including WordPress.com, WordPress VIP, WooCommerce, Jetpack, and Tumblr.

IPO plans. Home-sharing company Airbnb Inc. expects to go public next year, the company said Thursday. The company didn't release other details about an initial public offering. WSJ Pro "https://www.wsj.com/articles/airbnbs-first-quarter-financials-show-strong-growth-rate-cash-reserves-11565998183?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc" that Airbnb was planning to go public in the first half of next year, citing people familiar with the situation.

VC diversity. Almost half of employees at venture-capital funds in London say their companies are doing nothing to correct the under-representation of women and ethnic minorities in the industry, according to Private Equity News, citing a new report by Diversity VC.

Cloud IPO. Shares of Datadog Inc. jumped as they made their debut in the public markets, reflecting robust investor demand for companies tied to cloud-based technologies. The New York-based company's stock shot up 39% to $37.55. Index Ventures, OpenView Venture Partners and Iconiq Strategic Partners were among the company's biggest investors. Read more.

Exits

BallerTV, a provider of live amateur sports coverage, acquired FieldVision LLC, a mobile sports live-streaming service, for an undisclosed amount. Pasadena, Calif.-based BallerTV is backed by investors including Index Ventures, Peak Ventures, Unusual Ventures, Chernin Group, Moving Capital and the USC Marshall Venture Fund. FieldVision is listed in the portfolios of Precursor Ventures, Chaac Ventures and Lemnos.

Shares of Exagen Inc. rose more than 32% in their trading debut Thursday after the diagnostic test maker's upsized initial public offering of 3.6 million shares was priced at $14 each, the low end of the expected range. Exagen's major investors included Sun Mountain Capital, Tullis Health Investors, Hunt Investment Group and H.I.G. Capital

Tech News

Cash may be king in India, but Google is prince of mobile payments. Millions of Indian consumers are using Google Pay to spend and transfer money.

Zuckerberg heads to Capitol Hill to push his version of internet regulation. Facebook's founder will seek to placate lawmakers who are weighing tougher moves.

California enacts law to classify some gig workers as employees. Gov. Newsom signed a bill opposed by ridesharing companies but signaled hopes to broker compromise between tech giants and labor unions.

Yes, you can sleep on the job. Just please use the nap room. Companies in Japan are creating spaces to snooze, but workers say the premeditated nature of using them smacks of sloth.

Huawei launches first smartphone without Google's key apps. Chinese tech giant's Mate 30 series doesn't include apps such as Google Maps and YouTube following U.S. blacklisting.

Meanwhile, Huawei was suspended from a global forum aimed at combating cybersecurity breaches. A group formed to respond quickly to hacks and other cyber threats has temporarily expelled the Chinese company after legal advice.

Amazon to add 100,000 electric vehicles as part of climate pledge. CEO Jeff Bezos says the company plans to be carbon neutral by 2040 and plans to meet the goals of the Paris climate agreement 10 years early.

AT&T explores parting ways with DirecTV. The telecom giant considers the fate of its DirecTV satellite unit as cord-cutting saps its subscriber base.

Around the Web

Two top technology investors cautioned against drawing broad conclusions about the valuation of private companies from questions swirling around office-sharing startup WeWork. (Reuters)

Could mimicking evolution give rise to more sophisticated AI? (MIT Technology Review)

A radical idea to make computing more efficient would involve mimicking the probabilistic behavior of quantum mechanics. (Nature)

Renewable energy is so cheap that the handouts they once needed are disappearing. (Bloomberg)

New Money

Ginkgo Bioworks Inc., a Boston-based producer of microbes and other biological ingredients for industrial customers, secured $290 million in Series E funding from new and existing investors including funds and accounts advised by T. Rowe Price Associates. The company's backers include General Atlantic, Viking Global, Y Combinator, Cascade Investment and Felicis Ventures.

Checkr Inc., a San Francisco-based company that provides background checks of on-demand workers, raised $160 million in Series D funding at a valuation of $2.2 billion. T. Rowe Price led the round, which included participation from Accel, Y Combinator, IVP, Bond and Coatue.

Boundless Bio, a La Jolla, Calif.-based cancer therapeutics startup, closed a $46.4 million Series A round. ARCH Venture Partners and City Hill Ventures co-led the investment, with participation from Vertex Ventures, GT Healthcare Capital Partners, Boxer Capital of the Tavistock Group, Alexandria Venture Investments and others.

Built Robotics Inc., a San Francisco-based developer of artificial-intelligence guidance systems to transform construction equipment into autonomous robots, completed a $33 million Series B round. Next47 led the funding, with Partner T.J. Rylander joining the company's board. Building Ventures, Founders Fund, New Enterprise Associates, Lemnos and Presidio Ventures also invested in the round.

Simple Feast, a Copenhagen-based startup that delivers weekly boxes of prepared plant-based meals in biodegradable packaging, picked up $33 million in Series B funding. 14W led the round, with participation from investors including Balderton Capital.

Wunder Mobility, a Germany-based provider of carpooling, smart shuttle and fleet management technology, added $30 million in Series B financing, bringing the round total to $60 million. Investors including Blumberg Capital and KCK Group provided the funding.

AppLearn, a digital adoption platform with offices in the U.K. and San Francisco, snagged a $25 million commitment led by K1 Investment Management.

HappyOrNot, a Finland-based creator of a feedback system that allows organizations to gauge consumer and employee satisfaction levels, raised $25 million in growth financing. Verdane led the round and was joined by Northzone.

Darkstore, a San Francisco-based one-hour and same-day urban delivery service, scored $21 million in Series B funding led by EQT Ventures.

Versantis, a Zurich-based startup developing disruptive therapies for rare liver and pediatric diseases, nabbed a 16 million Swiss franc ($15.8 million) Series B round. Swisscanto Invest by Zrcher Kantonalbank led the investment, which included contributions from Esperante Ventures, investiere, Redalpine HealthEquity, Zrcher Kantonalbank Start-up Finance and others. Robert Schier of Swisscanto Invest will join the board.

Tethr, an Austin, Texas-based conversational intelligence platform, closed a $15 million funding round. IAG Capital Partners led the investment, which included participation from GroTech Ventures, MissionOG and Falmouth Ventures. In connection with the financing, Gary Clark was promoted from chief operating officer to chief executive of the company.

Robin Healthcare, a Berkeley, Calif.-based provider of a digital physician assistant, obtained an $11.5 million Series A round led by Norwest Venture Partners.

Additionally, a New Money item in Tuesday's newsletter incorrectly stated that Cloudian secured a $9 million Series A round. The investment was raised by Edgematrix, which is a newly launched subsidiary of Cloudian.

Mr. Crossick joins the company from Hotels.com, where he was most recently vice president of global product. Mr. Lakhani was previously chief operating officer and CFO at OfferPad. Salad Chain Sweetgreen's Tech Focus Helps Push Valuation to $1.6 Billion; Hedge-fund firms bankroll restaurant chain's expansion and food-delivery plans

By Katie Roof 
595 words
20 September 2019
20:48
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Investors are putting another $150 million behind Sweetgreen Inc., betting that the salad restaurant chain's embrace of technology will deliver them an exit in a sector that has seen minimal venture activity in recent years.

The Culver City, Calif.-based company said it was valued at $1.6 billion in the financing round, up from a $1 billion valuation when it last raised capital 10 months ago. A pair of hedge-fund managers, Lone Pine Capital and D1 Capital Partners, led the round, with participation from True Ventures and other existing investors.

Sweetgreen first raised outside capital early this decade, when the food sector was gaining some traction with venture investors. Some venture capitalists had shied away from bricks-and-mortar businesses, preferring to fund startups with internet-based business models. Others saw an opportunity to fund fast-growing brands.

Yogurt shop Pinkberry Inc. and sandwich shops the Melt and Potbelly Corp. also raised funding around the same time, and Blue Bottle Coffee Inc. and Philz Coffee Inc. became popular with venture investors, drawing comparisons to the early days of Starbucks Corp., when it was backed by venture capital.

A few of these companies have managed to exit. Potbelly went public in 2013, and Nestl SA"https://www.wsj.com/articles/nestle-buys-majority-stake-in-premium-coffee-chain-blue-bottle-1505407881" Blue Bottle in 2017. Private-equity firm Riverwood Capital invested in the Melt in 2013.

Jon Callaghan, co-founder of True Ventures, invested in Blue Bottle and hopes to replicate some of that success with Sweetgreen. He acknowledged it is difficult to create a differentiated business in the food category, but he said Sweetgreen's customer loyalty and incorporation of technology distinguish it from other salad shops.

"Today's consumer really wants the brand to mean something," Mr. Callaghan said. Sweetgreen represents "incredible fidelity to the supply chain, incredible commitment to sustainability," he said.

Jonathan Neman, Sweetgreen's co-founder and chief executive, said most purchases at its "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sweetgreen-salad-new-store-model-tech-incubator-11563280524" are made through its smartphone app. He said he expects that to continue when the company begins offering deliveries through its app next year.

The move will put Sweetgreen in the same category as several delivery apps that have raised venture funding, including Postmates Inc. and DoorDash Inc. However, Sweetgreen will focus on delivering its own food and will remain on Uber Eats and other delivery apps.

Sweetgreen also uses digital products to forecast sales, deploy labor and order food, Mr. Neman said. The technology is primarily driven by data and machine learning, he said.

"We don't consider ourselves just a salad place," Mr. Neman said.

Kelly Granat, a portfolio manager at Lone Pine Capital, said she invested in Sweetgreen because she is optimistic about the company's growth.

"We're investing here with the hopes that this will be a public company one day," she said.

Sweetgreen plans to use the new capital to launch its app-based delivery program and to set up more designated delivery locations in office buildings. The company also plans to open new retail locations in Miami; Denver; and Austin, Texas.

The latest funding round brings the company's total amount raised to more than $500 million since it was founded in 2007. Fidelity Investments led a $200 million round last November at a $1 billion valuation. Other prior venture investors include Revolution and Collaborative Fund.

Happy Friday! This morning's news that Nordic Capital has sold a stake in itself should hardly surprise WSJ Pro readers. After all, firms have been selling stakes in themselves at a steady pace for years now, including recent deals involving BC Partners, HGGC, GI Partners and Siris Capital. But as Will Louch writes this morning, what sets the Nordic deal apart is the buyer of its stake, Ottawa Avenue Private Capital, a unit of RDV Corp., the family office of the DeVos family. Although certainly not a newcomer to private equity, Ottawa Avenue is hardly a name that commonly pops up as a buyer of GP stakes. Could this be a future source of capital for other GPs eager to sell stakes in themselves?

Meanwhile, WSJ Pro's Laura Cooper reports that the initial public offering of Ping Identity Holdings isn't just a milestone for the cybersecurity company but also for its private-equity backer Vista Equity Partners.

Now on to today's news...

Today's Top Stories

DeVos Family Office Enters Market for Buyout Firm Stakes

The latest deal in the booming market for private-equity firm stakes has gone to an unusual buyer: the DeVos family office. Ottawa Avenue Private Capital, a subsidiary of RDV Corp., which serves the extended family of U.S. Education Secretary Betsy DeVos and her husband, Richard, is set to "https://www.wsj.com/articles/devos-family-office-affiliate-to-buy-stake-in-nordic-capital-11568973601?mod=djemPrivateEquityPro&tpl=pe" in European private-equity firm Nordic Capital, according to people familiar with the matter. Financial details of the transaction couldn't be learned, but the deal is expected to be made public later this week, WSJ Pro's Will Louch reports.

Ping Identity Listing Marks IPO Milestone for Vista Equity Partners Listing Marks IPO Milestone for

Ping Identity Holding Corp.'s initial public offering "https://www.wsj.com/articles/ping-identity-listing-marks-ipo-milestone-for-vista-11568933532?mod=djemPrivateEquityPro&tpl=pe" not only in the company's past three years of private-equity ownership, but also for its backer, Vista Equity Partners. Ping, which made its debut on the New York Stock Exchange Thursday, is the first Vista portfolio company to go public since the firm's launch in 2000, WSJ Pro's Laura Cooper reports. The IPO valued the company at $1.16 billion, nearly double the $600 million a person familiar with the company said Vista paid for it in 2016. The shares closed at $20.11 Thursday, rising about 34% from the offer price of $15.

Big Number

68%

The proportion of private-equity fund investors that plan to target small- to midmarket buyout funds for future investments in the asset class, according to Preqin Ltd.

Deals

Founded in 1940 in Roccaforte Mondov, Italy, the company produces sparkling beverages and bottled mineral water. DeA Calital's IDeA Taste of Italy fund invested in the company in 2017. DeA Capital in Milan has about  ¬12 billion in assets under management, according to its website.

Lovell Minnick Partners has offered 315 pence a share to acquire Charles Taylor PLC, an insurance services company based in London, according to a regulatory filing with the London Stock Exchange. The company said the offer represents a 34% premium over its closing share price of 235 pence on Wednesday. The offer puts a value of 261 million ($326.8 million) on the deal, the filing shows. While the company's directors intend to support the bid unanimously, it requires the assent of at least 75% of shareholders at a special meeting expected to be held in November as well as regulatory approvals, according to the filing. Based in Radnor, Pa., Lovell Minnick managed about $3.5 billion in client assets at the end of last year, according to a U.S. regulatory filing.

HarbourVest Partners has acquired a minority stake in Mesa Natural Gas Solutions LLC for $52 million. Mesa provides field generators that convert natural gas coming out of oil wells into electric power for use by nearby energy companies. Mesa is a portfolio company of Dallas-based private-equity firm BP Energy Partners.

Caisse de dpt et placement du Qubec has invested 53 million Canadian dollars ($39.8 million) in Neuvoo Inc., an employment listings website and one of the fastest-growing companies in Canada. The Montreal company plans to use the invested capital to finance international expansion and development of its artificial intelligence-based product that inventories job openings from across the internet.

Knox Capital has invested in A360inc, a Carrollton, Texas, technology and outsourcing services provider to the financial services, real-estate and legal industries in the creditors' rights market. Knox is an affiliate of Morgan Stanley Private Credit and ORIX Mezzanine & Private Equity.

Grede, which makes specialty iron castings and machine components for the automotive, commercial vehicle and industrial markets, generated $781 million in sales in 2018.

Crossplane Capital has acquired a controlling interest in Accent Family of Cos. The Tomball, Texas, company makes and distributes building materials and wire products for the waste recycling and other industries. Blackstone Group Inc. is exiting its stake in NCR Corp. in exchange for $302 million in cash and 9.16 million shares of common stock valued at about $308 million on Wednesday, the Atlanta-based maker of automated teller machines said. The New York private-equity firm sold roughly half of its NCR stake in early 2017, Dow Jones Newswires reported.

Mr. Ball most recently was senior vice president of distribution services at AmerisourceBergen Drug Corp. and remains a health-care industry consultant. Ms. Vallone has served in a variety of senior roles at software-as-a-service companies, including as president and chief executive of WebCT Inc., Wimba Corp. and Meteor Learning, and remains a director or trustee of several medical institutions in the Boston area.

Stephen Schwarzman:  I like to do things that are beautiful 

Lionel Barber 
2,461 words
20 September 2019
12:00
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

The Blackstone co-founder on risk-taking, Trump whispering   and why the rich shouldn t pay more tax

When Stephen Schwarzman originally proposed lunch, he suggested two venues: Claridge s or St Tropez. Naively, I assumed there was a choice. A rendezvous on the Cte d Azur, where Schwarzman enjoys one of his many palatial residences, was always a tad risky for a man who loves to control the situation.

Invited for 12.15pm, I spot Schwarzman standing centre stage in The Foyer & Reading Room restaurant. Short and tanned, he s wearing a lightly pinstriped dark suit with his favourite combination: blue-striped shirt with white collar and purple tie. He s put on a few pounds since he was a top runner in high school and college, but at 72 he still looks trim.

Encounters with Schwarzman are rarely dull. He s a combative character who, while outwardly charming, does not take kindly to criticism. We ve exchanged expletives in Davos and in his luxuriously appointed office at Blackstone, the Wall Street private equity and asset management firm he co-founded 34 years ago. But there have never been any hard feelings.

Schwarzman s fortune (net worth about $18bn) has bought him power and influence. He s graduated from being a mega dealmaker to philanthropist, back channel in US-China relations and  Trump whisperer . I want to explore these multiple roles, but also pin down why the man who has built one of the most successful financial businesses on the planet has never quite received the credit he believes he deserves.

A Wall Street rival offers a clue:  Steve is one of the most honest people I know. Most people lie to your face. He ll always tell you straight up he s going to screw you. 

As we study our menus, I raise the character question. His restless, driven nature is legendary.  Yes, it s true,  he admits.  It goes with the territory, the person.  As if on cue, he announces we re moving table because of a cold draught, compounded by noisy neighbours.

Schwarzman says, half-joking, that unlike his younger twin brothers he inherited  the right gene mix  from his mother,  a very formidable, strong survivor . His father by contrast was  a lovely, super-bright but not ambitious person  who ran a linen store in Philadelphia.  We could have been Bed Bath & Beyond. I don t know why not. That s the way my mind has always worked, he just had no interest. Fascinating. 

My guest is not given to self-doubt. Accepted at Yale, he phoned the dean of admissions at Harvard to parley his way in. (He was rejected.) After Harvard Business School, he joined Lehman Brothers. He always coveted the top job, but the infighting was murderous and he ended up selling the failing bank to American Express. He struck out on his own, co-founding Blackstone in 1985 with Pete Peterson, the former Lehman chairman and Nixon-era commerce secretary.

Our waiter arrives bearing water. Schwarzman orders tomato and basil soup, followed by poached lobster salad. I choose smoked salmon and grilled Dover sole off the bone. Alcohol is out because I am on dry August and my guest says he only drinks wine sparingly. (Schwarzman takes an anticoagulant pill a day to ward off phlebitis, a vein inflammation that killed his grandfather.)

Blackstone started as a boutique advisory firm, with the goal of making enough money to start its own private equity fund. Private equity has attracted controversy because of alleged asset-stripping: buying companies, loading them with debt ( leverage ) and selling them off at a handsome profit, with favourable tax treatment.

Schwarzman casts himself as a long-term investor, not a scavenger in sheep s clothing. He recounts with gusto the megadeals and the risk-taking involved in picking the right time to buy and sell assets, ranging from US Steel s railroad network to the Waldorf hotel.

His skill is market timing. Blackstone has expanded into real estate and hedge funds and other  alternative assets , with $545bn under management today. Blackstone funds are also the largest owner of real estate in the world. The firm s rise epitomises the  buy side  revolution that favours asset managers at the expense of traditional banks trading liquid securities.

Schwarzman has written a book, which is part memoir, part Blackstone management primer called What It Takes: Lessons in the Pursuit of Excellence. (A better title would be Whatever It Takes, I suggest.) There are many valuable insights; perhaps the biggest is the list of endorsements featuring Henry Kissinger, Eric Schmidt, the ex-Google chairman, Janet Yellen, the ex-Fed chair, and Mark Carney, governor of the Bank of England.

Peterson and Schwarzman worked together for 33 years, but the father-son relationship fell apart at the end. Peterson felt he had been squeezed out of his fair share of an expanding business. Schwarzman argued that he was the rainmaker, the firm was hiring more people and something had to give.  He had reached a different stage in his career and he volunteered to reduce his interest in the firm and did. 

This is the unsentimental side of Schwarzman. He appears not to care. He prides himself on his intuition (Blackstone went public ahead of the Great Crash in 2008 and, with fresh capital, positioned itself perfectly for the rebound). He also highlights his ability to recruit and retain high-quality executives such as Tony James, his longtime number two, and Jon Gray, the real estate guru, both fellow billionaires.

Blackstone is a meritocracy, he says, where two iron rules apply: no internal politics and do not lose money.  I go from the premise that anybody of talent does not want necessarily to be a private in an army. The lowest they want is to be a lieutenant colonel and preferably they d all like to be generals. 

Hang on, I say, there s no mistaking who s generalissimo at Blackstone. Schwarzman still signs all cheques above $250,000, retains a veto on major decisions and later declares:  I don t think I ll ever retire. My grandfather never did. 

Anyway, surely someone loses money some time, I add.

He half-concedes the point:  Every once in a while there s some weird fucking thing where government does something or there s a technological breakthrough that s transformative that a reasonable person couldn t know what is going to happen. And so that introduces what I call unpredictable risk, and you can get, potentially, picked off by that. 

I ask Schwarzman how his lobster tastes.  Pretty good,  he replies, picking at the pink flesh. My Dover sole is fresh and tasty. So how does Schwarzman assess the state of modern capitalism?

 Well, I would answer that with another question: what s the state of modern politics? 

The  rejiggering  of governments in the west, he argues, is a legacy of the financial crisis, globalisation and a massive transfer of jobs and wealth to emerging markets such as China. And that s before the impact of technology. These trends have squeezed the middle class and lower-income groups.

His remedies start with an increase in the minimum wage to about $15, up from single digits. This will destroy some jobs, but it will directly benefit 15 to 20 per cent of the population, with a knock-on effect on compensation for another 20 per cent, he argues.

Schwarzman wants to dramatically improve education levels in the US. He would make teachers tax exempt so as to raise wages and create  a new privileged class . Two-thirds of America s workforce went to high school or less, he says.  This is not the workforce of the future. 

Finally, he would encourage the Baby Boom generation that s retiring to take up jobs as mentors and assistant teachers. (In a pre-lunch gesture, he offered to donate all book proceeds to Lucy Kellaway s like-minded charity, Now Teach. The offer was accepted.)

How about the mega rich paying more taxes? Schwarzman looks incredulous.  Taxes on the wealthy? 

Well, why not?

 The tax system in the US is extremely progressive 	. 	. 	. 	it s a reverse pyramid. The top 10 per cent pays 70 per cent [of the tax take]. The top 1 per cent pays 38 to 40. I pay around 48 to 47 per cent tax rate.  (He later clarifies this is a  blended rate  covering ordinary income, capital gains and dividends.)

Schwarzman can be tin-eared. He once compared a proposal to tax private equity executives selling their publicly listed stock as ordinary income to Hitler invading Poland. He says it was a private conversation, but does not deny railing against the  semi-socialist  Obama administration. Today, socialism is no longer a dirty word in the Democratic party. Is he worried about the 2020 presidential election?

 The politics of anger and free things is a powerful potential elector,  he says, adding that the US is more polarised than ever in his lifetime.  Vietnam was about one issue. This is much more intense, much more far-reaching. 

The gravity of the conversation demands light relief, happily provided by the waiter, who congratulates Schwarzman on his choice of the upside down strawberry tart as dessert.

Waiter:  It s the new style. 

LB:  New style? 

Waiter:  Yes sir. 

LB:  What s the old style?

Waiter:  A regular strawberry tart. 

Schwarzman won t talk on the record about Donald Trump, a wearily familiar response from chief executives, even billionaires. Yet as chairman and chief executive of Blackstone, he s running the firm while also advising Trump. He says the potential conflicts of interest are manageable, but there have been awkward moments, especially after he had to disband the chief executive panel advising Trump in the wake of far-right violence in Charlottesville in 2017.

Did Trump equivocate about the far-right?  Most of us had no idea there were Nazis in America. That may mean we re naive 	. 	. 	. 	We also had no idea there was something called anti-fascistas who also used violence. The whole thing was completely disorienting. 

Trump, he concludes, did not get his words right first time. The second time, it  looked right . By the third time, the US had experienced  a society-galvanising moment .

Schwarzman has been accused of wearing too many hats. In China, where Blackstone has important business, he s tried to be a bridge builder; but he agrees that relations between Beijing and the US are at a low point.

 China has enjoyed the most stunning transformation in the past 40 years of any country, most probably, in world history. It did it through a variety of mechanisms that are not consistent with the rules and regulations of the developed world. 

Now, in effect, the US is asking Beijing to adopt western practices. This, he admits, is very difficult. But, he suggests, the Chinese leadership has been told that however difficult they think Trump is, the Democrats could be far worse in terms of trade protectionism.

We turn to Schwarzman s generous philanthropy. In recent years, he has donated $100m to the New York Public Library, $150m to Yale, 150m to Oxford university, $350m to MIT. He s also set up the Schwarzman scholars, a one-year masters programme on global affairs for top international students to study at Tsinghua University in Beijing. (Full disclosure: I have served on the 200-strong panel of judges for the past four years.)

Schwarzman says he only wants to donate to causes that are  paradigm shifting , such as the artificial intelligence lab at MIT. But he is a hard-boiled negotiator when it comes to disbursing money and naming rights. The New York Public Library became so frustrated that they proposed putting his name on the roof (that way his name would be seen by every plane flying over Manhattan to La Guardia). The funds came through. (Schwarzman s name is now on every entrance to its main building.)

The ego has landed in many other prestigious locations. His 60th birthday party in 2007, featuring Rod Stewart in Manhattan s Armory, was reported to have closed Park Avenue ( It was only one lane,  he protests). His 70th was an equally extravagant affair with an ancient Silk Road theme held at a five-star hotel near President Trump s Mar-a-Lago estate in Florida.

Why is glitz and showiness so integral to Steve Schwarzman?

 That s a good question,  he says, attributing his aesthetic taste partly to his second wife, Christine.  I like generally doing things that nobody has done. I also like to do things that are beautiful. 

He points to the striking glass sculpture in the Foyer, with writhing snakes hanging like a chandelier. This was part of the refurbishment of Claridge s, a trophy picked up when Blackstone bought the Savoy Group 21 years ago.  I actually went out to the state of Washington and met with the sculptor Dale Chihuly to check it out. 

Schwarzman wonders aloud how lucky he has been   a rare moment when the scrappy self-made man lets himself go. I note that we are meeting on the 100th anniversary of the death of Andrew Carnegie, the Scottish-born steel magnate, one of the world s greatest philanthropists who gave away his entire fortune (about $300bn at today s prices).

 Do you agree with Carnegie when he said: he who dies rich dies disgraced? 

Schwarzman offers a rambling answer about America s  tax infrastructure . So will he follow Warren Buffett s rallying call to the rich to donate more than 50 per cent of their fortune to charity? (Buffett himself has gone higher, to 99 per cent).  Maybe I will, maybe I won t, but to me that s not relevant 	. 	. 	. 	obviously I ve given it a lot of thought and the vast amount will go to charity. 

Two and a half hours have passed. I switch off the tape recorder. Schwarzman looks slightly relieved.

Just as I m preparing to settle the bill, Wall Street s arch dealmaker pulls out a copy of the FT and points to an ad highlighting the longlist of the McKinsey-FT business book of the year.

 What It Takes did not make the list. Is it really too late? 

Whatever it takes.

Lionel Barber is editor of the FT

Good day. We report on new funds from five firms in today's newsletter, demonstrating the breadth of the venture industry's reach and its continued ability to raise more capital.

Data Collective raised $725 million to invest in deep technology-related startups, while publicly traded Splunk Inc. launched a corporate venture arm called Splunk Ventures with two funds: a $100 million Innovation Fund and a $50 million social impact fund.

Meanwhile, Revolution Ventures closed its latest $215 million fund to invest in startups outside of the main coastal tech centers. In the News

SoftBank's New York move. SoftBank Group Corp. plans to open a New York office for its Vision Fund, marking the global investor's fifth base of operations and positioning it to tap more actively into the city's startup scene, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/softbanks-vision-fund-to-open-new-york-office-11568838843?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc".

* The New York office will open soon, according to Lydia Jett, a SoftBank partner, who was speaking Wednesday at a conference in the city hosted by seed firm Primary Venture Partners. It isn't clear who will lead the new office or when it will open. Ms. Jett declined to comment further.

* The first Vision Fund has invested in several New York companies, including real-estate companies We Co., which this week postponed its IPO plans, and Compass, which raised $370 million in July.

* A New York office would expand the fund's global network of outposts beyond its current offices in London; San Carlos, Calif.; Tokyo; and Singapore.

Undruggable no more. Seed investors are backing a startup that sees machine learning as the key to enabling drugs aimed at molecular targets that have long been thought to be out of pharmaceutical companies' reach, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/startup-aims-to-unlock-drug-discovery-with-machine-learning-11568823688?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc". Unnatural Products Inc. secured more than $6 million in seed financing to fund its mission of solving the problem of "undruggable" proteins the roughly 80% to 90% of proteins in the body that have eluded drugmakers. Molecules called macrocycles have shown the ability to disrupt protein-protein interactions, but drug developers historically have needed some serendipity to identify ideal macrocycle drugs. Using machine learning, Santa Cruz, Calif.-based Unnatural seeks to optimize macrocycles to turn them into effective medicines.

Own together. Some well-funded startups have an unusual pitch for homeowners strapped for cash: Let's own this house together. A handful of companies, including those backed by marquee Silicon Valley names such as Andreessen Horowitz and Mark Zuckerberg's philanthropic organization, are experimenting with a product that essentially lets them take an ownership position in a house along with the homeowner, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/silicon-valley-explores-a-new-investment-your-home-1518955201?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc".

More scrutiny. Foreign investors who want to put money into U.S. businesses that rely on sensitive technology, infrastructure and data could face greater national-security scrutiny under proposed rules released by the Trump administration, the Journal's Katy Stech Ferek reports. Treasury Department officials provided a first glimpse of how they would implement Congress's order last year to expand the scope of national-security reviews of foreign investment deals, including those involving satellites, oil refineries, financial-market systems and drinking water utilities.

Subpoena time. The leader of a House committee investigating Juul Labs Inc. said the e-cigarette maker has failed to turn over documents it requested and warned that the committee could issue a subpoena demanding them, the Journal's Micah Maidenberg reports.

Ping Identity Listing Marks IPO Milestone for Vista; Offering for the cybersecurity company marks the first time a Vista Equity portfolio company went public since the firm's launch

By Laura Cooper 
529 words
19 September 2019
23:52
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Ping Identity Holding Corp.'s initial public offering marked a milestone not only in the company's past three years of private-equity ownership, but also for its backer, Vista Equity Partners.

Ping, which made its debut on the New York Stock Exchange Thursday, is the first Vista portfolio company to go public since the firm's launch in 2000.

Shares of Ping, which priced in the middle of their range at $15 each, rose by around 34% to $20.11 each by the end of its first day of trading.

The IPO valued the company at $1.16 billion, nearly double the $600 million a person familiar with the company said Vista paid for it in 2016.

Austin, Texas-based Vista will retain ownership of around 80% of the company following the IPO, according to a regulatory filing.

Ping, of Denver, provides identity management products to more than 1,200 customers in sectors that include retail, banking, health care and manufacturing. Many businesses enlist Ping to help modernize their identity security infrastructure, according to founder and Chief Executive Andre Durand.

Under Vista's ownership, Ping expanded its reach and product offerings through two acquisitions. In 2016, it bought UnboundID, a customer-identity and access-management software company. Two years later, its acquisition of Elastic Beam broadened Ping's reach into the artificial intelligence world by acquiring a product that uses AI to identify and block cyberattacks.

The company's annual revenue rose 17% to $201.6 million last year from $172.5 million in 2017, according to a filing with the Securities and Exchange Commission.

Mr. Durand said that Ping decided to pursue a possible public offering about a year to a year-and-a-half ago.

"Vista appreciated how important Ping was to the enterprises we were serving," said Mr. Durand, adding that it was at that time the company and its private-equity backer talked about growing through an IPO.

Vista built a strong record exiting portfolio companies through sales to strategic buyers or to other private-equity firms. The firm had previously planned another IPO on the London Stock Exchange three years ago with Misys Group Ltd., a financial services software provider, but later "https://www.wsj.com/articles/misys-scraps-london-ipo-1477585989" because of to market conditions. The company has since merged with D+H to form London-based Finastra, another Vista portfolio company.

Although it is still too early to tell how Ping's shares will perform over the long run, demand for cybersecurity technology and services across the globe has boosted valuations for such companies, both in the public and private markets.

Another cybersecurity company, SailPoint Technologies Holdings Inc., which was backed by tech-focused private-equity firm Thoma Bravo, has seen its share price grow by at least 72% to more than $20 each since it "https://www.wsj.com/articles/lbo-focus-thoma-bravo-hopes-sailpoint-can-ride-post-ipo-tailwinds-1514548800?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=3". SailPoint provides on-premises and software-as-a-services identity and access management solutions.

Business
Wall St rivals seek research clients beyond fund industry

ROBIN WIGGLESWORTH 
336 words
19 September 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
21
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Cross asset

Goldman Sachs and Morgan Stanley are going head-to-head with the likes of Bain and McKinsey, hoping to sell research services to companies to offset big falls in demand from their traditional clients in asset management.

Historically, the reams of research and economic analysis produced by Wall Street's army of "sellside" analysts has been targeted at hedge funds and fund managers the "buyside" in industry jargon.

But investment groups have come under ferocious fee pressure in recent years and are trying to cut down on costs. At the same time, new regulations stemming from the EU and which have washed over the US have required banks to charge investors for the research they provide, rather than bundling the cost into commissions for trading.

As a result, fund managers have slashed budgets for spending on research, spurring banks to look for new opportunities in the corporate world.

Simon Bound, global head of research at Morgan Stanley, said: "The catalyst is pressure on the overall business. These are incremental dollars we didn't think about before, that we are now trying to bring into the firm."

The motivation is somewhat different at Goldman Sachs, where chief executive David Solomon has been aiming to build relationships with executives at smaller companies, hoping that they can boost the bank's investment banking revenues.

Steve Strongin, the bank's global head of research, said: "The shift is very real. There is a broader recognition that research can be useful to our clients ... beyond the investment industry."

Research divisions within banks often have staffing and resources that dwarf the biggest think-tanks and consultancies, churning out huge amounts of industry, economic and financial research, from simple stock recommendations to machine learning-driven analyses of corporate sentiment. Total commissions paid by US fund managers, covering both research and trading, came to almost $8bn last year, according to Greenwich Associates, down from a peak of $14bn in 2009.

News
Deutsche Bank bets on German tech start-up

OLAF STORBECK 
304 words
19 September 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Usa
14
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Financials

Deutsche Bank is paying  ¬50m for a 4.9 per cent stake in open-banking start-up Deposit Solutions in a transaction that values the Hamburg-based venture at more than  ¬1bn.

The deal turns Deposit Solutions into Germany's most highly valued fintech after Berlin-based lender N26, which in July was valued at $3.5bn when it raised $170m from its investors.

The start-up operates software that links connects retail clients and lenders, allowing banks to collect deposits from retail clients across Europe who are not their direct customers. It is a rival to Berlin-based Raisin, which, among other investors, is backed by PayPal.

Deposit Solution's valuation more than doubled since its last financing round in August 2018, when it raised $100m from Munich-based private equity group Vitruvian Partners and other investors.

For Deutsche Bank, which also owns stakes in financial analytics start-up Finanzguru, the transaction is the largest fintech investment in its history. The lender will pay roughly half of the  ¬50m in cash.

It has also committed to provide Deposit Solutions with in-kind services that both partners value at  ¬25m, according to a person familiar with the transaction.

Since 2017, the German lender has been one of the first and biggest clients of Deposit Solutions. Its software is at the core of Deutsche Bank's "Zinspilot", a tool that Deutsche's 7m retail customers in Germany can use to funnel savings to third-parties such as real estate lender Deutsche Pfandbriefbank and Cerberus-owned My Money Bank.

Germany's largest lender is planning to roll out the Deposit Solutions software to the 13m clients of its Postbank brand, its 4m retail clients outside Germany, as well as its small business and wealth management customers.

Moody's Downgrades J.Crew on Madewell Plan

Good day. Donald Trump made a fundraising trip to Silicon Valley Tuesday, with former Sun Microsystems Inc. Chief Executive Scott McNealy hosting the event, The Wall Street Journal reported.

It's the first time since his election that Trump has visited the Bay Area. Three years ago, complaints from Intel employees caused the chief executive of Intel at the time to cancel an event for Trump.

Silicon Valley hasn't been a bastion of support for Trump. He has sought to restrict immigration and free trade, which puts him at odds with many in the technology world. But there are some notable supporters, according to a Vox report.

Note: Please check out our new, redesigned home page. It includes all the same great news and information, but with a new look and feel and streamlined navigation. Let us know what you think.

And now on to the news...

In the News

Software development. GitLab Inc., a fully remote company that sells tools for software developers within a single application, has raised $268 million, thanks to strong demand for its products from corporations, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/software-development-startup-gitlab-raises-268-million-11568725200?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc".

* The latest influx of cash brings the startup's total funding since 2015 to $426 million and its valuation to $2.75 billion, said Sid Sijbrandij, co-founder and chief executive. GitLab's newest investors include Adage Capital Management LP and Altimeter Capital Management LP.

* The company plans to go public in November 2020, Mr. Sijbrandij said.

* Propelling growth at GitLab, incorporated in 2014, is a shift in how companies' information-technology teams develop software, with a focus on collaboration between various departments and fast and frequent deployments, the CEO said.

Crypto custody. What is the safest way to store digital currencies? The answer to that question could decide the biggest winners in a multibillion-dollar race to determine what companies will be the leading custodians of cryptocurrencies, which are increasingly being used by money managers and other institutions for themselves or their clients, WSJ Pro's Tomio Geron reports. As with traditional assets, such as stocks, bonds or gold, SEC-registered investment advisers are required by law, with certain exceptions, to hold assets with a qualified custodian, a specialized financial firm that holds a customer's assets separate from the customer's financial manager or brokerage firm. For cryptocurrencies, the role of custodian is especially important, since all a thief or hacker needs to steal cryptocurrency is the string of private keys, or codes, assigned to that specific asset.

French unicorns. French insurers and asset managers have pledged  ¬5 billion in investment for homegrown tech firms as part of President Emmanuel Macron's push to nurture France's fledgling startups into a cohort of highly valued heavyweights, the Journal's Noemie Bisserbe reports. Mr. Macron announced the funding on Tuesday before hosting a dinner for executives from about 40 sovereign funds and venture-capital firms including KKR & Co., Accel and Lightspeed Venture Partners at the lyse Palace on Tuesday evening. Investors include AXA SA, BNP Paribas's insurance unit and Natixis SA, according to French officials.

Robotic surgery. CMR Surgical Ltd. said it has raised $240 million in a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-k-s-cmr-surgical-raises-240-million-to-launch-robot-surgery-technology-11568742328?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc" to prepare for the imminent launch of its robotic surgery technology. The U.K.-based business said Tuesday the new financing puts its valuation at more than $1 billion. It was backed by a number of existing and new investors including LGT, Escala Capital Investments, Cambridge Innovation Capital, Watrium and Zhejiang Silk Road Fund. The funding will go to commercialize CMR's Versius product a robotic instrument that enables surgeons to perform minimally invasive surgeries through the use of a digital interface.

Drug bankruptcy. Sienna Biopharmaceuticals Inc. has filed for bankruptcy with plans to find a buyer or investor during its chapter 11 proceedings, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/sienna-biopharmaceuticals-files-for-bankruptcy-11568746455?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc". The publicly traded drugmaker, whose major investors include Arch Venture Partners, filed for bankruptcy days after its cash levels fell below the minimum prescribed by a credit agreement, a court filing shows. Advent Raises $2 Billion for Debut Technology Fund; The global private-equity firm has opened an office in Palo Alto, Calif., and hired seven professionals for its tech team

By Will Louch 
393 words
18 September 2019
15:38
WSJ Pro Private Equity
RSTPROPE
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Advent International is the latest private-equity firm to bet big on technology.

The firm said Wednesday it had raised $2 billion for its debut fund dedicated to investments in software companies and data centers across North America and Europe.

Advent joins a number of buyout shops that have raised large funds to invest solely in technology companies. Vista Equity Partners, led by billionaire Robert Smith, earlier this month collected $16 billion for what stands as the largest technology-focused fund ever raised by an independent private-equity firm.

Investor appetite for tech-focused funds pushed total capital raised for such vehicles in North America to $122.1 billion through Sept. 5, already surpassing the $116.2 billion raised during all of 2018, according to data provider Preqin Ltd.

Advent plans to invest as little as $50 million in equity per deal from the fund, called Advent Global Technology, but it could invest up to $2 billion or more if it also taps into its latest flagship fund, which closed with $17.5 billion earlier this year.

The firm plans to back a range of transactions, including minority growth investments, traditional buyouts and acquisitions of public companies. So far it has backed two deals from the new fund: Definitive Healthcare LLC, a U.S. health-care data analytics company; and payment solutions provider Transaction Service Group.

To help source deals, Advent said it has opened a new office in Palo Alto, Calif., and added seven new professionals to its tech team. The new hires bring the team to 20 professionals, including former TPG Partner Bryan Taylor, who joined Advent in February. Advent said it plans to add more people to the team in the coming months.

In addition to the new Palo Alto office, the firm also has tech-focused deal makers in its London and New York offices.

Founded in 1984, Advent is one of the world's largest private-equity firms. The firm has invested in more than 345 private-equity transactions in 41 countries. It had $54 billion in assets under management as of June 30.
