Banking & Finance: Credit Unions Go On Buying Spree For Small Banks

By Lalita Clozel 
871 words
4 September 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B12
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

WASHINGTON -- Credit unions are buying small banks in record numbers, a trend that is prompting pushback from the banking industry.

Credit unions have acquired 21 U.S. banks since 2018, according to S&P Global Market Intelligence, compared with 12 purchases in the prior five years.

Bankers say the deals are proof credit unions are growing too aggressively given their not-for-profit business model. Credit unions say they are driven by the same market forces that have driven hundreds of bank mergers in recent years.

"We see opportunities to expand our network, bring in new products to the table, and get a good return on investment," said Brian Wolfburg, the president and chief executive of VyStar Credit Union in Florida, which closed its first bank acquisition in August.

Banks have complained for decades that credit unions, which have tax-exempt status, compete with an unfair advantage over financial products such as mortgages, credit cards and business loans while benefiting from weaker regulatory requirements. Banks have lost numerous battles to change regulation and tax treatment of credit unions. Credit unions say their growth helps them extend lower-cost loans and better deposit rates to more consumers.

"Credit unions serve the greater good, not the greater greed," said Dan Berger, the president and chief executive of the National Association of Federally-Insured Credit Unions, a trade group.

Credit unions, owned by member-customers, are exempt from corporate taxes and certain lending rules. But they also must serve lower-income households and limit customers to a restricted pool of people who generally work together or live near one another.

Credit union and bank tie-ups represent a tiny fraction of the consolidation happening in the industry. There were 187 mergers between credit unions approved in 2018, according to the National Credit Union Administration, compared with nine announced deals between credit unions and banks.

So far this year, credit unions have announced mergers with 12 banks.

Credit unions also are smaller overall, holding $1.5 trillion in assets in the first quarter of 2019, compared with $18 trillion for banks and thrifts.

Still, the deals illustrate how competitive credit unions have become, given their tax advantages, lack of shareholders and diminishing constraints on growth.

"The substantive mission-driven aspects of the credit union charter that are supposed to make them unique and justify many taxpayer benefits have eroded, if not ended," said Karen Shaw Petrou, whose firm Federal Financial Analytics Inc. published a June report on credit unions commissioned by the American Bankers Association.

Last year, Martin Steele hired an investment bank to field potential buyers for his $206 million-asset firm, The Bank of Washington.

Mr. Steele, the Seattle-area bank's president and chief executive at the time, said he received six offers. He chose the one from Sound Credit Union, in part because it was an all-cash bid and he worried the value of competing stock-based offers from banks could drop before a deal closed.

"I've got five friends who I'm saying no to, and I've got a credit union who I've never met before who I'm saying yes to," said Mr. Steele, who now serves as president and chief executive of First Sound Bank.

Laura Lee Stewart, president and chief executive of Sound Community Bank in Seattle, was one of the bidders that had an existing relationship with Bank of Washington. Ms. Stewart, who is also chairman-elect of the ABA, said the community will lose a tax-income source as a result of the deal.

"When an entity is in business doing the same thing and gets a free ride, that's bad public policy," she said. "And it's bad for communities."

Sound Credit Union, based in Tacoma, Wash., said the acquisition would allow it to expand its services to customers and grow to new markets.

"We believe that together, our combined knowledge and experience will better serve our members and communities," Don Clark, president and chief executive of the firm, said in a statement. He added that Sound Credit Union had paid $3 million in payroll, property and sales taxes in 2018.

Credit unions face limits to how they can structure the deals. They must ensure that the customers of the bank they are acquiring will fit into their field of membership and that they won't hit a business-lending limit as a result of the acquisition.

NCUA Chairman Rodney Hood said he plans to introduce a rule later this year to clarify credit unions' regulatory responsibilities when acquiring banks.

Credit unions should "make sure that they are acquiring a bank that comports with their existing field of membership and the lines of business they are operating in," Mr. Hood said in an interview. The NCUA and bank regulators generally need to approve these deals before they close.

To get around laws prohibiting credit unions from holding bank charters, many of the deals are structured as "purchase and assumptions," in which the bank empties out its loans and deposits and transfers everything over to the credit union.

The Property Report
Foreigners Pull Out Of Market --- Overseas investors fear U.S. commercial property has hit peak as they shift assets

By Esther Fung 
838 words
4 September 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B6
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

A strong appetite among foreign investors for office buildings, apartments, malls and other real estate has in part fueled the long-running bull market in U.S. commercial property.

Now, amid a maturing property market cycle and rising uncertainties in geopolitics and the global economy, foreign investors have sold more U.S. commercial real estate than they bought in a quarter for the first time since 2013.

After years of amassing huge portfolios, investors from abroad sold $13.4 billion of property in the second quarter of 2019, according to data firm Real Capital Analytics. During the quarter, foreign investors purchased $12.6 billion of real estate.

European and Canadian investors have been active sellers recently, along with some high-profile investors from China.

"U.S. real estate is priced very high right now, and people think we're close to the top of the market," said Matt Posthuma, a partner in Ropes & Gray's asset management practice.

The waning appetite of foreign investors for U.S. property contrasts with their intensifying interest in other asset classes.

Foreign money managers still see U.S. assets as a haven and have been piling into U.S. stocks and bonds, buying nearly $64 billion in these assets in June, the largest sum since August 2018, according to data from the Treasury Department.

What's the difference? Compared with buildings, stocks and bonds are easier to buy and sell. If a recession hits, property owners may find it more difficult to sell their assets.

"For someone looking out three to five years, their investments may not be worth as much as they are today," Mr. Posthuma said. "That's the fear."

Some domestic investors in commercial property also are moving to the sidelines. Overall, sales volume of commercial U.S. property fell 9% in the first quarter of this year from one year ago and rose a modest 2% in the second quarter to $127 billion.

For decades, sovereign-wealth funds, insurers, pension funds and family offices across the world have sought assets in the U.S. to diversify their investments and seek steady returns. Many also are drawn to the market's relative stability, vast pool of consumers and deep and liquid market.

But, with the dollar strong, overseas investors unloading U.S. assets can chalk up foreign-exchange gains against weaker local currencies along with profits from higher prices. The strong dollar also means buying U.S. property has become more expensive for them.

Some investors are shunning investments in flood-prone areas, as rising sea levels and hurricanes amplify costs to mitigate damages, particularly in older buildings.

Finding good deals also has become trickier in the U.S. as a possible recession looms and other regional and economic trends -- including the impact of e-commerce on the retail sector -- cast a shadow over some property types.

One of the biggest foreign sellers during the second quarter was Ivanhoe Cambridge, the real-estate arm of Canadian pension fund Caisse de depot et placement du Quebec, which completed the sale of $2.2 billion worth of properties, according to Real Capital.

In the second quarter, Ivanhoe Cambridge sold two office towers in Seattle and its stake in Ritz Plaza, a 479-unit rental apartment building in Midtown Manhattan.

"It's investment discipline," said Sylvain Fortier, chief investment and innovation officer at Ivanhoe Cambridge. "When you reached the level you set out at the beginning, don't get greedy. Get out and recycle your money into something else."

The Quebec pension fund is focusing more these days on industrial real estate and less on other property types. Late last year, Ivanhoe Cambridge invested in IDI Logistics, an Atlanta-based developer and operator of warehouses.

In another megadeal in June, Singapore-based investment manager GLP agreed to sell a U.S. network of industrial warehouses, totaling 179 million square feet, to Blackstone Group LP for $18.7 billion, the largest private real-estate transaction ever. GLP has said it would keep a small footprint in the U.S. and is still looking to expand but didn't provide a timeline.

Overseas investors still interested in the U.S. real-estate market are also starting to structure their acquisitions differently.

They are choosing to invest in debt rather than equity, which is seen as a safer bet, if the economy slides closer to a recession.

They are also taking on more minority stakes to lower the chances of triggering a review by the Committee on Foreign Investment in the U.S., the country's national-security panel that vets foreign investment in U.S. businesses.

Last year, Congress expanded the authority of the panel, known as Cfius, which has hindered some real-estate transactions if they include land located near a military installation or other sensitive U.S. government property.

Business News: Psychedelic-Drug Treatments Draw Investors, Skeptics

By Heather Mack 
497 words
4 September 2019
The Wall Street Journal
J
B5
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

Psychedelic substances are gaining legitimacy as a treatment for mental illnesses, prompting a handful of entrepreneurs and venture-capital investors to look for business opportunities in the nascent sector.

Still, veteran health-care investors say it is too early to jump into the market given the legal ramifications. Psilocybin, found in "magic mushrooms," has been decriminalized in countries including the Netherlands and Portugal, but remains illegal under U.S. federal law.

"We would not invest in something where we were also betting that the illegality would change," said Bryan Roberts, a partner at Venrock Associates, one of the oldest venture-capital firms in the U.S. "But we would absolutely invest in something that was utilizing some novel understanding of biology and generating new research into drug development."

Partners from Canaan Partners and New Enterprise Associates, both longtime health-care and life-science investors, said the sector isn't even on their radar.

Acceptance in the U.S. is growing. Denver effectively decriminalized psychedelic mushrooms in May and Oakland, Calif., followed in June. Research suggests psychedelics could help treat clinical depression, post-traumatic stress disorder and other conditions.

A few large, well-known startups based in Europe have attracted high-profile investors.

Germany-based ATAI Life Sciences AG is using a big-data approach to psychedelic-drug development. U.K.-based Compass Pathways Inc. last year received approval from the U.S. Food and Drug Administration to run clinical trials to test the use of psilocybin on patients with treatment-resistant depression.

ATAI is the largest investor in Compass. Collectively, the two companies have raised about $122 million from investors including billionaires Peter Thiel and Michael Novogratz.

But some investors and entrepreneurs believe there are many more opportunities to come, as no major pharmaceutical company is developing psilocybin-based drugs.

"There are a lot of reasons to believe this could be the next big thing," said Ronan Levy, who co-founded Field Trip Ventures Inc. this year to invest in startups developing compounds and therapy protocols with psychedelics. "There is a strong grass-roots movement, a general acceptance of the safety profile, and a growing acceptance of the therapeutic value."

Firms including Field Trip Ventures, Numinus and Tabula Rasa Inc. -- all based in Canada, where psychedelics are illegal at the federal level -- also say they believe interest from the private sector will help the push for drug-policy changes.

Numinus, founded this year, has acquired a clinic in Vancouver and is working with psychiatrists and therapists to design psychedelic treatment protocols as well as software to analyze efficacy.

Other companies are staying away from pushing legalization efforts or presenting their offerings as health care. Amsterdam-based Synthesis Retreats and Jamaica-based Atman Retreat, which operate in countries where psilocybin use is legal or decriminalized, run wellness retreats catering to people who haven't been diagnosed with clinical illnesses.

Trade War's Impact Grows; Consumers Drive U.S. Growth; Australia Central Bank Stands Pat

1,753 words

Good day. The impact of the U.S.-China trade war is widening, with more small businesses in the U.S., manufacturers in Asia and others feeling the effects as a new round of tariffs kicked in. Meanwhile, U.S. consumer spending and inflation picked up in July, but the prospects for continued strong spending ahead are less clear. And Australia's central bank left rates unchanged.

Now on to today's news and analysis.

Top News

U.S.-China Trade War's Global Impact Grows

The escalating trade war between the U.S. and China is rippling through the global economy, hurting confidence among U.S. small businesses, crimping trade among industrial giants in Asia and hitting export-oriented factories in Europe. On Sunday the U.S. imposed fresh tariffs of 15% on Chinese goods including clothing, tools and electronics. A round of retaliatory Chinese tariffs also took effect, targeting imports of U.S. soybeans, crude oil and pharmaceuticals. Beijing said on Monday it lodged a complaint with the World Trade Organization over the Trump administration's tariffs.

* Small Businesses' Faith in Economy Hits Low on Tariff Uncertainty

* Trump: Companies, Not Trade Policies, to Blame for Business Setbacks

* Trade Disputes Curb Global Factory Activity as Confidence Wanes

Strength in U.S. Consumer Spending Drives Economy

U.S. households "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-consumer-spending-rose-sharply-in-july-11567168319?mod=djemCentralBanksPro&tpl=cb" in July, providing reassurance that the economy's decadelong expansion continued to roll despite slowing factory activity and global growth. Personal-consumption expenditures, a measure of household spending, increased a seasonally adjusted 0.6% in July from June, a pickup from the previous two months, the Commerce Department said Friday, continuing a solid performance by the economy's main driving force.

* Cheap Gas Revs Up Consumers, Boding Well for U.S. Economy

* Analysis: The American Consumer Keeps Beating Expectations

Inflation Picked Up in July

U.S. inflation rebounded in July on the back of strong consumer spending, the latest sign that price pressures are recovering from a slow start to the year that helped motivate the Federal Reserve to ease borrowing costs. The Fed's preferred inflation measure, the personal consumption-expenditures price index, rose a seasonally adjusted 0.21% in July from the prior month and 1.38% from July 2018, the Commerce Department said.

Other Developments Around the World

Australia's RBA Stands Pat But Leaves Door Ajar to Cut Further

The Reserve Bank of Australia left its cash rate steady at a record low of 1% at a policy meeting Tuesday, but left the door open to cut interest rates further in coming months if the job market weakens and global shocks threaten growth.

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/glynns-take-australias-economy-very-likely-contracted-in-2nd-quarter-11567411322?mod=searchresults&page=1&pos=14&mod=djemCentralBanksPro&tpl=cb"

The Market Forces That Propelled a Massive Rally in Long Bonds

Long-term bonds have been on a tear in recent weeks with yields tumbling enough to heighten fears of a possible recession ahead. But the bond market's closely watched signals may have become exaggerated.

* One Obscure Reason for August's Big Bond Rally: Negative Convexity

Germany's Small Stimulus Steps Unlikely to Meet Economists' Hopes

German officials say the government is preparing measures to support demand, but they are likely to fall well short of the big stimulus the world is hoping for. Faced with a shrinking economy, the finance ministry has been working on proposals worth about  ¬50 billion ($55 billion) and almost entirely financed by debt, according to two senior officials.

Argentina's President Pulls a U-Turn, Imposes Capital Controls

Facing financial-market turmoil, Argentina's government on Monday implemented capital controls in an unexpected move that comes more than three years after President Mauricio Macri lifted similar restrictions.

* Peso Strengthens Against Dollar After Currency Controls Introduced

Historic Asset Boom Passes by Half of American Families

The decadelong economic expansion has showered the U.S. with staggering new wealth driven by a booming stock market and rising house prices. But that windfall has passed by many Americans. The bottom half of all U.S. households, as measured by wealth, have only recently regained the wealth lost in the 2007-2009 recession and still have 32% less wealth, adjusted for inflation, than in 2003, according to recent Federal Reserve figures.

Financial Regulation Roundup

Risky Seller Financing Flourishes Where Homes Are Cheapest

Poorer Americans buying inexpensive homes have few options in the conventional mortgage system. That has pushed them to a "contract for deed" a financing option full of pitfalls and prone to abuse.

Venture-Capital Stalwart Battles Washington's Crypto Crackdown

Venture-capital firm Andreessen Horowitz, known for making early investments in innovative companies such as Facebook, is putting up a fight against Washington's cryptocurrency crackdown.

U.S. Unveils Rules on Forgiving Student Loans After School Fraud

The Trump administration will resume canceling the student-debt obligations of Americans who prove that their colleges defrauded them, under a long-awaited plan unveiled Friday.

CFOs Say They Underestimated Lease Accounting Challenges

Many U.S. public companies underestimated the difficulties of complying with a new lease-accounting standard, finance chiefs say. Their stumbles could inform private companies that are now preparing for the transition.

Fraudsters Used AI to Mimic CEO's Voice in Unusual Cybercrime Case

Criminals used artificial intelligence-based software to impersonate a CEO's voice and demand a fraudulent transfer of funds in March in what cybercrime experts described as an unusual case of AI being used in hacking.

India Merges State Banks to Battle Bad Debt

India said it was merging several state-run banksas it seeks to shore up the banking system amid concerns about a growing bad debt problem. Finance Minister Nirmala Sitharaman said the move was aimed at reducing costs and streamlining operations. After the merger, India will have 12 state-run banks instead of 18.

In Zimbabwe, Promise of Mobile Money Fades When Power Goes Out

Ecocash, the cellphone platform used for eight out of 10 transactions in Zimbabwe, regularly breaks down, making buying milk, filling up a car or settling a utility bill a test of endurance. "This country has gone to the dogs," complains one man after a three-hour attempt to buy gas.

Regulator Files Complaint Against Danske Bank

Denmark's Financial Supervisory Authority said Friday it has reported Danske Bank A/S to the police for misleading its customers with one of its investment products.

Commentary

Japan's Sales-Tax Increase Threatens a Labor-Market Miracle

"After a multiyear boom, Japan's job market may now be as strong as it's going to get. That's not good news, since tumbling unemployment hasn't yet generated solid wage growth, and the sales tax is set to increase to 10% from 8% in just a month's time," writes WSJ's Mike Bird, who calls the Oct. 1 tax rise a needless economic mishap. "The move is all but guaranteed to dent consumption again, and given the country's already-tenuous economic growth, could easily spark another recession. Rock-bottom interest rates mean that Japan's net interest spending is among the lowest of any advanced economy, and fiscal consolidation is unnecessary. With the labor market now looking like it may have reached its peak without generating stronger consumption, fighting the phantom threat of the country's high debt load is a mistake" Prime Minister Shinzo Abe shouldn't make again, Mr. Bird says.

Basis Points

* U.S. household sentiment fell in August from the earlier month amid concerns over a trade war. The University of Michigan on Friday said its final index of August consumer sentiment was 89.8, a drop of 8.6 points from July and the largest monthly fall since December 2012.

* The Canadian economy rebounded in the second quarter as one of the strongest export showings this decade offset a decline in business investment and soft household spending.

* India's economic growth slowed to a six-year low last quarter as debt problems at banks choked business and consumer spending in Asia's third largest economy.

* South Korea's consumer inflation slowed to a record low last month, pushing the index further below the central bank's 2% target.

* The chances of an early British election to resolve the Brexit impasse appeared to rise on Monday as Prime Minister Boris Johnson launched a broadside at lawmakers seeking to thwart his Brexit plans.

* Argentina's presidential front-runner said the government's new plan to restructure its short-term debt shows the country is virtually insolvent, as a crisis of confidence has wiped out private-sector demand for government debt.

* The euro fell to a two-year low Friday as some investors bet that lackluster economic data would force the European Central Bank to further ease monetary policy next month.

* Turkey's economy expanded in the second quarter but remained smaller than it was a year earlier, data released Monday showed, a challenge for President Recep Tayyip Erdogan, whose approval ratings have slumped amid high unemployment and inflation.

Business
Asset managers must clamp down on their pay

Jane Fuller 
772 words
2 September 2019
Financial Times
FTFT
1; Main
9
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Has the fund management industry become a force for good in reining in executive pay? Evidence from the UK suggests that the upward march has moderated with last year's 13 per cent fall as a cause of celebration.

While votes on pay at annual meetings tend to receive more than 90 per cent shareholder approval, dialogue between investors and remuneration committees before the vote will have helped curb excess.

Two crucial questions are now being danced around. The first is whether institutional shareholders are really concerned about absolute pay levels and the gap between the top and the average worker. The second is whether a big driver of differences between the two bonus payments is justified.

The proof on absolute pay will be whether last year's reduction turns out to be more than a dip related to falls in equity markets. A report from the High Pay Centre, a think-tank that monitors pay in top roles, and Chartered Institute of Personnel and Development, anHRindustry association, shows that it is too early to pronounce a downward trend. The median pay for FTSE 100 chief executives has been bobbling about in the 3.4mto4.2mrange since2010.

UK regulations on the pay gap, enacted in 2018, require large listed companies to report the ratio of chief executive pay to the median employee. The gap varied by sector, from 34 times in technology to 177 times in consumer goods last year, according to the report. Boards will have to refer to this ratio from next year in discussingexecutive pay.

The information will provide plenty of grist for commentary on fairness. To what extent will fund managers use it to influence board behaviour? Their own ratio will be much nearer the bottom of the range than the top, thanks to relatively flat hierarchies.

The less comfortable reason is that they have a lot of highly paid staff, including ones paid more than the chief executive. This jars with the changingworldfor activemanagers.

Fees have been driven down by competition from passive funds, lower returns. and transparency. The pressure is on to reduce costs, and staff are the biggest single one.

This brings us to the second difficult issue for the sector: bonuses. At Schroders, chief executive Peter Harrison's 6.17m of variable pay was 11 times his fixed salary   his bonus attracted a 12 per cent protest vote at the annual meeting.

Even at Legal & General Investment Management, which operates through passive funds and promotes so-called inclusive capitalism, senior managers' variable pay was 250 per cent of their fixed salary, according to disclosures to theFCA.

The cap for bankers under rules covering material risk takers is 100 per cent, or twice that with shareholder approval.

The cultural attachment to bonuses is rooted in the tradition of active management and the pursuit of alpha. This has the advantage of reducing fixed costs in a bad year. But while pay for performance will always have a place, separating out individual contribution, discounting for risk and choosing a timeframe for measurementremainproblematic.

The star culture can be seen as a feature of equity bull markets, where brilliant individuals dig into corporate accounts to gain an edge. In fixed income, with a narrower risk-reward trade-off, investment decisions more clearly involve a team, which draws onmacroandmicrosources.

Count in the growing cohort of technology experts, analysing data from satellites and natural language processing, and teamwork becomes vital for all asset classes. Are there any individual stars in the ETF market among those who devise screens to capture clients' values or who perfect tradingautomation? Another change has been the setting of non-financial goals for managers, such as executing group strategy and nurturing talent. But these are part of the job covered by base salary. The approach could be extended along the investment decision-making chain, recognising the broader dutiesof serving clients.

A final reason for the sector to rely less on variable pay is its sheer complexity. Bonuses are deferred and partly paid in shares or fund units whose value varies. They are subject to potential penalties and the discretion of remuneration committees . Valuing uncertain rewards is hard, and may even lead an individual to ask formore tocompensate.

The time has come for the sector to reviewitspayideology,makingsalary the most important element of the totalandflexing those totalsdown.

Jane Fuller is a fellow of CFA Society of the UK and co-director of the Centre for the Study of Financial Innovation

Guide to pricing of Authorised Investment Funds.

Compiled with the assistance of the IMA. The Investment Management Association, 65 Kingsway, London WC2B 6TD. +44 (0)20 7831 0898. All funds within this section, whether OEICs or unit trusts are authorised in the UK by the Financial Services Authority. The prices quoted should only be used as a guide.

OEIC: Open-Ended Investment Company. Similar to a unit trust but using a company rather than a trust structure.

Share Classes: Separate classes of share are denoted by a letter or number after the name of the fund. Different classes are issued to reflect a different currency, charging structure or type of holder. Buying price: Also called offer price. The price at which units in a unit trust are bought by investors. Includes manager's initial charge. Selling price: Also called bid price. The price at which units in a unit trust are sold by investors.

Single price: Based on a mid-market valuation of the underlying investments. The buying and selling price for shares of an OEIC and units of a single priced unit trust are the same. Exit Charges: The letter E denotes that an exit charge may be made when you sell units, contact the manager/operator for full details. Time: The time shown alongside the fund manager's/operator's name is the time of the unit trust's/OEIC's valuation point unless another time is indicated by the symbol alongside the individual unit trust/OEIC name. The symbols are as follows:m0001 to 1100 hours;. 1101 to 1400 hours;.1401 to 1700 hours; # 1701 to midnight. Daily dealing prices are set on the basis of the valuation point, a short period of time may elapse before prices become available.

Yield: Funds comprising mainly of bonds normally quote a gross redemption yield after all charges but before tax has been deducted. Funds mainly made up of equities normally quote a yield representing the estimated annual payout net of tax for basic rate taxpayer. For further information contact the management company.

Historic pricing: The letter H denotes that the managers/operators will normally deal on the price set at the most recent valuation. The prices shown are the latest available before publication and may not be the current dealing levels because of an intervening portfolio revaluation or a switch to a forward pricing basis. The managers/operators must deal at a forward price on request, and may move to forward pricing at any time. Forward pricing: The letter F denotes that that managers/operators deal at the price to be set at the next valuation. Investors can be given no definite price in advance of the purchase or sale being carried out. The prices appearing in the newspaper are the most recent provided by the managers/operators. Scheme particulars, prospectus, key features and reports: The most recent report, scheme particulars, prospectus and key features document may be obtained free of charge from fund managers/operators.

WSJ Pro
Cyber Daily: Scam Draws on AI to Trick Executive Into Wiring Money

1,394 words
30 August 2019
13:26
WSJ Pro Cybersecurity
RSTPROCY
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Hello. The CEO of a U.K. company, fooled by an AI-enabled impersonation of the voice of his boss, wired money to a bank account, letting scammers get away with about $243,000, WSJ Pro's Catherine Stupp reports. The incident early this year shows that hackers are picking up basic artificial intelligence technologies for cyberattack. Traditional cybersecurity tools weren't built to detect AI-enabled voice impersonation, experts say.

Also today: U.S. prosecutors look into more instances of alleged tech theft by Huawei; General Electric to build new tech for Pentagon to protect military information and other data; Mount Sinai patients get notice their personal information was exposed.

Note: We're taking a break Monday for Labor Day in the U.S. Cyber Daily will be back on Tuesday.

Fraudsters Use AI to Mimic CEO's Voice in Cybercrime Case

Scams using artificial intelligence are a new challenge for companies

By Catherine Stupp

Criminals used artificial intelligence-based software to impersonate a chief executive's voice and demand a fraudulent transfer of  ¬220,000 ($243,000) in March in what cybercrime experts described as an "https://www.wsj.com/articles/fraudsters-use-ai-to-mimic-ceos-voice-in-unusual-cybercrime-case-11567157402?mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy&mod=djemCybersecruityPro&tpl=cy".

The chief executive of a U.K.-based energy firm thought he was speaking on the phone with his boss, the CEO of the firm's German parent company, who asked him to send the funds to a Hungarian supplier. The caller said the request was urgent, directing the executive to pay within an hour, according to the company's insurance firm, Euler Hermes Group SA.

Euler Hermes declined to name the victim companies.

Law enforcement authorities and AI experts have predicted that criminals would use AI to automate cyberattacks. Whoever was behind this incident appears to have used AI-based software to successfully mimic the German executive's voice by phone. The U.K. CEO recognized his boss's slight German accent and the tenor of his voice on the phone, said Rdiger Kirsch, a fraud expert at Euler Hermes, a subsidiary of Munich-based financial services company Allianz SE.

Several officials said the voice-spoofing attack in Europe is the first cybercrime they have heard of in which criminals clearly drew on AI. Euler Hermes, which covered the entire amount of the victim company's claim, hasn't dealt with other claims seeking to recover losses from crimes involving AI, according to Mr. Kirsch.

Scams using AI are a new challenge for companies, Mr. Kirsch said. Traditional cybersecurity tools designed to keep hackers off corporate networks can't spot spoofed voices. Cybersecurity companies have recently developed products to detect so-called deepfake recordings.

It is unclear whether this is the first attack using AI or whether there are other incidents that have gone unreported or in which authorities did not detect the technology in use, said Philipp Amann, head of strategy at Europol's European Cybercrime Center. Europol is the European police agency. While it is hard to predict whether there might soon be an uptick in cyberattacks using AI, Mr. Amann said hackers are more likely to use the technology if it makes attacks more successful or profitable.

Multistage attack

The attackers responsible for defrauding the British energy company called three times, Mr. Kirsch said. After the transfer of the $243,000 went through, the hackers called to say the parent company had transferred money to reimburse the U.K. firm. They then made a third call later that day, again impersonating the CEO, and asked for a second payment. Because the transfer reimbursing the funds hadn't yet arrived and the third call was from an Austrian phone number, the executive became suspicious. He didn't make the second payment.

The money that was transferred to the Hungarian bank account was subsequently moved to Mexico and distributed to other locations. Investigators haven't identified any suspects, Mr. Kirsch said.

It is unclear whether the attackers used bots to react to the victim's questions. If they did, it might have been even more difficult for law enforcement authorities to investigate, Mr. Amann said. A police investigation into the case has ended, Mr. Kirsch said. Europol wasn't involved.

Mr. Kirsch believes hackers used commercial voice-generating software to carry out the attack. He recorded his own voice using one such product and said the reproduced version sounded real.

A few software companies offer services that can quickly impersonate voices, said Bobby Filar, director of data science at Endgame, a cybersecurity company. "You don't need to be a Ph.D. in mathematics to use it." he said.

Another tactic hackers could use would be to stitch together audio samples to mimic a person's voice, which would likely require many hours of recordings. Security researchers demonstrated this technique at the Black Hat conference last year.

Attackers could use publicly available voice recordings to impersonate celebrities or executives.

"You can't go around and be silent the entire time. You're going to run into situations like this where you expose information that you never thought could be used against you," Mr. Filar said.

Applying machine-learning technology to spoof voices makes cybercrime easier, said Irakli Beridze, head of the Centre on AI and Robotics at the United Nations Interregional Crime and Justice Research Institute.

The UN center is researching technologies to detect fake videos, which Mr. Beridze said could be an even more useful tool for hackers. In the case at the U.K. energy firm, an unfamiliar phone number finally aroused suspicions. "Imagine a video call with [a CEO's] voice, the facial expressions you're familiar with. Then you wouldn't have any doubts at all," he said.

Big Number

$5 Trillion

Estimated annual world-wide cost of data breaches by 2024, up from $3 trillion today, according to Juniper Research Ltd. Increasing regulatory fines and lost business account for the majority of the tally.

More Cyber News

U.S. prosecutors probe Huawei on new allegations of technology theft. U.S. prosecutors are looking into additional instances of alleged technology theft by Huawei Technologies Co., The Wall Street Journal reports, citing people familiar with the matter, potentially expanding beyond existing criminal cases against the Chinese telecommunications giant. Among the situations being examined are episodes in which Huawei was accused of stealing intellectual property from multiple people and companies over several years, as well as how the company went about recruiting employees from competitors, these people said.

IP theft allegations: The new inquiries overlap with findings from a Wall Street Journal investigation in May that documented numerous allegations of intellectual property theft against Huawei throughout its history, these people said. They include alleged theft of smartphone-camera technology from a Portuguese multimedia producer and Huawei's practices of recruiting employees from rival companies, the people said.

Huawei said: At the time, Huawei said it is committed to complying with laws in global markets and has a track record of innovation. "We respect the integrity of intellectual property rights for our own business, as well as peer, partner and competitor companies," the company said. It couldn't be determined if new charges are being sought against the company. A Huawei spokesman declined to comment. A Justice Department spokesman also declined to comment.

GE to help Pentagon protect data moving between classified, unclassified networks. The Pentagon's Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency, or Darpa, gave General Electric Co. a contract for $8.6 million to build technology to isolate various parts of government networks, NextGov reports. The flow of some military information and other sensitive data now is controlled with air gaps, or breaks between systems or networks. GE, along with Galois Inc. and Northrop Grumman Corp., will work on Darpa projects to improve air gap techniques and to build new technology to better secure data.

Mount Sinai notifies patients of data breach. A cyberattack at American Medical Collection Agency resulted in the exposure of sensitive data about more than 33,000 patients of Mount Sinai, Becker's Hospital Review reports. At least one unit of the New York-based hospital group used American Medical for billing services. Laboratory Corp. of America, Quest Diagnostics Inc. and other health-care firms have also been affected by the incident at American Medical.

WSJ Pro
VC Daily: Muscle Investors Cash Out; The Problem With Machine Learning ; FTC Probes Juul's Marketing

1,487 words
30 August 2019
13:43
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Happy Friday. Using machine learning to help make venture-capital investment decisions is an attractive idea, since many investors' own companies often use the technology.

But machine learning doesn't actually work well for venture investing, according to Alessio Fanelli of 645 Ventures, because the information you need to perform such analysis, like revenue numbers, is hard to get hold of. In addition, machine learning relies on analyzing past patterns, and venture investing typically requires looking to massive changes or completely new platforms that aren't accounted for in past data.

New York-based 645 does rely on technology when scouting investments, but generally for simpler tasks, such as finding all the companies in a particular sector, Mr. Fanelli wrote. Several venture firms, including Correlation Ventures, Rocketship.vc and SignalFire, say they use machine learning and other related technologies to find and identify promising companies.

Note: The WSJ Pro VC newsletter won't be sent on Monday in observance of the U.S. Labor Day holiday. We'll be back on Tuesday.

And now on to the news...

In the News

Muscular funding. Biotechnology venture capitalists recently have sold a trio of companies with potential treatments for muscle diseases, a sector where startups historically haven't attracted much funding, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/venture-investors-score-with-muscle-disease-treatments-11567110479?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc".

* This week, Modis Therapeutics Inc. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/venture-investors-set-to-make-quick-returns-in-modis-therapeutics-sale-11566855736?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc" by Zogenix Inc. in a deal worth up to $400 million. Vertex Pharmaceuticals Inc. purchased Exonics Therapeutics Inc. in June for up to $1 billion, and Sarepta Therapeutics Inc. exercised an option to buy Myonexus Therapeutics Inc. in February, paying $165 million.

* Muscle diseases draw less attention than say heart disease or neurological disorders, but venture funding in the category has jumped recently, rising to a record $512.1 million in 2018 from $266 million the previous year, according to Dow Jones VentureSource.

* One reason: Venture firms and drug companies are investing heavily in treatments for rare genetic diseases. Several genetic diseases affect the muscles, and have limited treatment options.

Juul investigation. The Federal Trade Commission is investigating the marketing practices of e-cigarette startup Juul Labs Inc., the Journal's Jennifer Maloney reported, citing people familiar with the matter. FTC investigators are looking at whether Juul engaged in deceptive marketing, including by targeting minors or using influencers, some of the people said. Juul is minority-owned by Altria, the maker of Marlboro cigarettes. Tiger Global and venture-capital firm Tao Capital also hold stakes in the company.

California gig-worker law. Lyft and Uber offered a set of proposals to California lawmakers that they described as a compromise: a minimum wage of $21 an hour for time spent driving and picking up customers, a fund to pay for benefits such as paid sick leave, and the ability for workers to organize a group that could negotiate with numerous employers of drivers all together, rather than individually. They said if Assembly Bill 5, that would classify drivers for those and other similar companies as employees, becomes law and no other legislation addressing their concerns passes, the companies would put their resources behind a measure on next year's ballot featuring the proposals they are backing.

Latin America fund. Kaszek Ventures has raised $600 million for technology investments in Latin America. The firm closed on $375 million for early-stage fund Kaszek Ventures IV and $225 million for a later-stage vehicle, Kaszek Ventures Opportunity I. Both funds were oversubscribed, the firm said. Kaszek had sought to raise $500 million for the funds as one of several firms targeting Latin American startups, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/latin-american-venture-firms-angle-for-slice-of-global-funding-boom-11562954831?mod=djemVentureCapitalPro&tpl=vc".

Tech News

The first female space tourist on what we can do in orbit. Anousheh Ansari, the chief executive of the XPrize Foundation, says that storing data and growing human organs would be easier off Earth but moon vacations are a ways off.

Swiss regulator promises scrutiny of blockchain companies. Switzerland's financial regulator issued guidance on the money-laundering risks associated with blockchain technology following a call to action by a global standard-setter this year.

U.S. prosecutors probe Huawei on new allegations of technology theft. U.S. prosecutors are looking into additional instances of alleged technology theft by Huawei Technologies Co., according to people familiar with the matter, potentially expanding beyond existing criminal cases against the Chinese telecommunications giant.

Tesla lifts prices in China. Tesla Inc. raised its prices in China ahead of new Chinese tariffs on imported U.S. cars, increasing the pressure on the California electric-vehicle maker to fast-track production at its new Shanghai plant.

The missing piece of Amazon's New York debacle: It kept a burn book. Amazon.com Inc. executives cataloged in minute detail the insults they saw coming from New York politicians and labor leaders in their ultimately abandoned push for a second headquarters in the city.

Apple tightens privacy rules on Siri recordings after backlash. Apple Inc. said it will no longer automatically save audio recordings of customers' interactions with voice-driven virtual assistant Siri, the latest step as technology companies try to ease privacy concerns.

Democratic senators want answers about listings for unsafe merchandise on Amazon.com. Three U.S. senators wrote a letter to Amazon.com Inc. CEO Jeff Bezos asking him to take action to stop the sale of unsafe items and to ensure accurate warning labels on his company's giant sales platform.

Alphabet dips its toe into infrastructure investing. The white-hot world of infrastructure investing has attracted a notable new entrant: Alphabet Inc. The technology giant is teaming up with its subsidiary, Sidewalk Labs LLC, and Ontario Teachers' Pension Plan to launch Sidewalk Infrastructure Partners.

Dating apps are making marriages stronger. Researchers from the University of Chicago found that more than a third of U.S. marriages between 2005 and 2012 started online and that online couples have longer, happier marriages.

ReadMe, a San Francisco based startup that helps companies build out their API documentation and developer metrics, raised $9 million in Series A funding. Accel led the round, with participation from Y Combinator.

Urbvan, a Mexico City-based van-sharing transportation app, landed a $9 million investment. Kaszek Ventures and Angel Ventures led the round, with participation from DILA Capital and Liil Ventures.

Worximity Technology Inc., a Montreal-based provider of factory analytics technology, picked up a $6.25 million in Series A investment led by Marel. The company previously raised funding from W Investments and Fonds de solidarit FTQ.

Alto, a Nashville, Tenn.-based startup that streamlines the process for investors to add alternative assets to their IRAs, closed a $5.4 million seed round. Jefferson River Capital and Moment Ventures led the funding, and were joined by Foundation Capital, Sequoia's Scout Fund and Amplify.LA.

Knock Knock, a San Francisco-based mobile social gaming startup, closed a $4 million Series A round. March Capital Partners led the funding, with contributions from London Venture Partners, Raine Ventures and Ludlow Ventures. Gregory Milken, managing director at March, will join the board.

The Redwood City, Calif.-based company has raised about $160 million in venture capital since 2011 from investors including Mayfield, Menlo Ventures, Uncork Capital and GGV Capital.

WSJ Pro
Government Budgeting Software Startup OpenGov Raises New Funding; OpenGov raises $51 million of new capital from Weatherford Capital, 8VC and others

By Marc Vartabedian 
529 words
30 August 2019
19:58
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

OpenGov Inc., a startup that offers budgeting software for local and state governments, has raised $51 million to continue its expansion into governments across the country, according to Chief Executive Zac Bookman.

Weatherford Capital Management and 8VC led the Series D round, while Andreessen Horowitz and others participated in the transaction, Mr. Bookman said in an interview on Wednesday.

OpenGov, based in Redwood City, Calif., has built a software platform that helps governments organize, display and track budget information. Roughly 2,000 U.S. governments and public agencies use the software.

The cloud-based software allows customers to manage annual budget cycles, manipulate large data sets, and create visuals and infographics to share on social media, among other capabilities.

Mr. Bookman said OpenGov helps governments operate more efficiently and save money by quickly revealing budgeting inefficiencies. The software can also improve local transparency if governments decide to share spending information publicly.

"This is the nitty-gritty catacombs back-office stuff," Mr. Bookman said. "But this is where our money goes."

Mr. Bookman added that governments choose to make more of this information public when using OpenGov because it is displayed in digestible user-friendly ways.

An example of a metric OpenGov could show is overtime spending by the local police department during summer months.

OpenGov customers include the Idaho State Controller's Office and the Illinois State Treasurer.

In August, the state of West Virginia began using OpenGov software to streamline financial reporting between the state and its municipalities.

Another startup, ClearGov, Inc., offers similar software and has raised roughly $5 million from investors including Kepha Partners.

In an email, ClearGov CEO Chris Bullock said the company's software can turn complex financial data into easy-to-understand infographics that residents can use to see how their tax dollars are being put to use and compare their own communities with others.

Meanwhile, numerous venture- and private equity-funded startups are targeting corporations, nonprofits and the education sector with similar software platforms focused on budgeting and financial analytics. Centage Corp., for example, has raised roughly $24 million from investors including TVC Capital, according to CEO John Murdock.

OpenGov will use the new capital to expand its product line and for research and development.

Mr. Bookman said the majority of OpenGov's existing investors also participated in the fundraising.

Thrive Capital a "https://www.wsj.com/articles/startup-that-got-a-seat-at-white-house-roundtable-is-part-owned-by-kushner-family-1500045800" and venture firm run by Josh Kushner, whose brother Jared is an adviser and son-in-law to President Trump didn't participate in the Series D round, Mr. Bookman said.

Mr. Bookman said OpenGov only works with state and local governments and has no intention of expanding to the federal government.

OpenGov has raised $140 million since it was founded in 2012. The startup's board includes Andreessen Horowitz co-founder and general partner Marc Andreessen; Joe Lonsdale, an 8VC founding partner and co-founder of data-analytics startup Palantir Technologies Inc.; and former CEO of Cisco Systems Inc. John Chambers.

WSJ Pro
Data Analytics  Company ThoughtSpot Valued at $2 Billion; Investors including Lightspeed provide $248 million in late-stage funding

By Marc Vartabedian 
459 words
28 August 2019
12:00
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

ThoughtSpot Inc. has raised $248 million in late-stage funding, valuing the business data analytics company at $1.95 billion.

Lightspeed Venture Partners led ThoughtSpot's recent Series E round, with Sapphire Ventures and others participating in the deal.

The company's capital raise comes at a time when some of its peers have scored multibillion-dollar acquisitions

Companies such as Walmart Inc. and streaming service Hulu use ThoughtSpot's software to enable non-tech-savvy users to pull up business and financial analytics in much the same way people type questions into Google search bars.

For example, a retailer could ask, "How many Nike shoes did we sell in these three ZIP Codes last quarter," Chief Executive Sudheesh Nair wrote in an email.

ThoughtSpot's search tool incorporates artificial intelligence to guide users in formulating questions in ways that will yield the most accurate results. The AI-based search also can offer up data patterns that users didn't necessarily know to ask about, Mr. Nair added.

Hulu employs ThoughtSpot to measure whether a particular show drove subscriptions. The streaming service uses the software to make content decisions that could increase subscriber growth and reduce churn, ThoughtSpot spokesman Ryan Mattison said. De Beers Group uses ThoughtSpot to chart pricing trends in the diamond trade, Mr. Mattison added.

ThoughtSpot charges customers by the amount of data they input into the software, said Mr. Mattison. Other startups, including Wolfram Alpha LLC and Arcadia Data Inc., have developed similar enterprise data tools.

The enterprise data analytics sector has attracted investor interest and notched recent acquisitions as companies aim to squeeze business insights out of the data they collect.

Alphabet Inc.'s Google unveiled a deal in June to acquire Looker Data Sciences Inc., a business-intelligence software and big-data analytics platform, "https://www.wsj.com/articles/google-to-acquire-data-analytics-firm-looker-for-2-6-billion-11559829249" in cash. Looker has raised about $280 million from venture firms including Redpoint Ventures and First Round Capital.

That same month, Salesforce.com Inc. agreed to buy data-analytics platform Tableau Software Inc. "https://www.wsj.com/articles/salesforce-to-buy-analytics-platform-tableau-11560167718" in stock.

Revenue for business analytics services companies is expected to grow 12% this year to nearly $190 billion, according to a report in April by International Data Corp., a market intelligence firm.

Ravi Mhatre, a partner with Lightspeed, said ThoughtSpot's ability to give decision makers at companies the ability to drum up instantaneous insight is valuable, adding that ThoughtSpot's tools enable executives to generate customized data insights quickly.

Based in Sunnyvale, Calif., ThoughtSpot has raised $554 million since it was founded in 2012.

Peloton wants IPO investors to believe it can kill the gym

Alice Hancock in London and Eric Platt in New York 
864 words
28 August 2019
20:15
Financial Times (FT.Com)
FTCOM
English
Copyright 2019 The Financial Times Ltd. All rights reserved. Please do not cut and paste FT articles and redistribute by email or post to the web. 

Route to becoming the Netflix of exercise is becoming more crowded

 One more effort 	. 	. 	. 	find that beat!  shouts Cody Rigsby, a Peloton instructor, from the 22-inch screen of one of the company s $2,245 exercise bikes.

The seven-year-old exercise company is now also trying to work investors into a lather after filing for an initial public offering later this year on the Nasdaq that it hopes will raise at least $500m.

Peloton s $4bn valuation, at the time of its last fundraise, rests on the idea that it does not just sell fashionable bikes, but that it is a media company, a Netflix of exercise, pumping out live and pre-recorded content to subscribers who believe it is a better proposition than gym classes.

The company says it produces more than 950 videos a month from its studios in New York and London, which can be watched not only on bikes but also on phones or its new treadmill. A subscription costs $39 a month in the US or 39 in the UK.

Frederic Court, founder of Felix Capital, which invested in Peloton when it first expanded out of the US in 2018, said that he had  never seen a company with so much customer love .

 There is so much enthusiasm from the user around the product. That is quite rare to see such a commitment. 

Peloton s growth has boomed, along with the wider fitness market. In September 2015, one year after it released its first bike, the company had attracted about 11,000 monthly subscribers.

Today, it counts 511,202, plus a further 102,000 subscribers to its digital boot camp, yoga and running classes who do not own a Peloton bike or treadmill, according to its prospectus. The group says it has so far only tapped a fraction of its addressable market, which it pegs at 67m households.

According to a 2018 report from the International Health, Racquet & Sportsclub Association, 183m people have gym memberships worldwide. Peloton is optimistic that a significant share of those members will swap their gym cards for a home bike or treadmill.

But its prospectus shows how expensive its marketing campaign has been, and while revenues more than doubled in the year to June 30   rising to $915m from $435m the year before   its losses rose nearly-four fold to $196m.

The listing also comes at a time when consumers are being flooded with fitness and wellness apps, hacks and memberships. Investors are wary that too much capital has flowed into the industry, allowing companies to pursue growth over profits. According to the most recent report from the Global Wellness Institute, the fitness and meditation industry was worth $595bn in 2017.

Peloton sees its main rivals as high end gyms and the likes of SoulCycle and Flywheel, which have both found cult-like followings for their high intensity cycling classes. It now has 2.4 times as many riders as SoulCycle, which pulled its own plans for an IPO, according to data from the analytics company Second Measure.

SoulCycle and its high-end gym operator owner, Equinox, were also recently hit by calls for a boycott because they are owned by Stephen Ross, the Donald Trump supporting property developer.

Peloton itself is locked in litigation after it used music in some of its beat-heavy fitness videos that music publishers allege were not properly licensed. When the company removed some of those classes from its catalogue, it encountered backlash from its riders.

It faces other companies encroaching on the fitness at home market. Mirror, for example, offers fitness classes beamed out of a mirror-like screen on your wall. It reached a $300m valuation in June. Zwift also offers a virtual cycling experience.

Paul Davies, head of leisure research at Mintel, has also warned that users outside of the US do not appear as keen on the company and roughly 98 per cent of Peloton s equipment sales have been in America.

 Awareness of Peloton is actually very, very low in the UK. There are a lot of people out there who probably aren t aware that you can pay for streamed workouts at home. I think part of this investment will be about boosting the brand,  he said.

John Foley, Peloton s founder who told the FT in a 2015 interview that it was  bone crushingly hard  to raise money ahead of its launch in 2014, wrote in the prospectus that Peloton  sells happiness .

But as the company identified in the risks to the business outlined in the S-1 filing, it will join a role call of lossmaking tech companies, including Uber, Lyft and Pinterest which could put investors off.

 We have incurred operating losses in the past, expect to incur operating losses in the future, and may not achieve or maintain profitability in the future,  it said.

But one source close to the company said that he did not think they would find it hard to attract investment.  To date they haven t struggled for investors,  they said.

Risk & Compliance Journal
C Suite
SEC Questions More Companies About Sanctions Disclosures; The regulator has sent letters to companies such as PayPal and BNY Mellon with queries about their dealings in countries targeted by U.S. sanctions

By Mengqi Sun and Mark Maurer 
1,004 words
28 August 2019
19:50
The Wall Street Journal Online
WSJO
English
Copyright 2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved. 

The Securities and Exchange Commission is increasingly questioning public companies about compliance with U.S. sanctions as the nation's blacklist of individuals and entities grows.

The regulator has slowly ramped up comment letters to companies seeking more details about disclosures related to dealings in countries targeted by U.S. sanctions, according to a Wall Street Journal analysis of regulatory filings and data compiled by consulting firm Audit Analytics Inc.

At least 42 companies received letters from the SEC last year regarding activity in areas subject to sanctions enforced by the Treasury Department's Office of Foreign Assets Control. That matches the 2017 total, which is up from an average of about 33 during the three years ending in 2016, according to Audit Analytics.

In recent months, the SEC has sent letters to companies such as PayPal Holdings Inc., Bank of New York Mellon Corp. and Chinese travel-services provider Ctrip.com International Ltd. with sanctions-related questions.

The SEC doesn't play an active role in penalizing companies for potential sanctions violations, but it does police companies' disclosures to ensure adequate reporting on business risks, said Scott Flicker, a partner at law firm Paul Hastings LLP in Washington, D.C.

"Companies make disclosures when they deem that information would be material for investment decisions, by current or potential investors," Mr. Flicker said. "Financial materiality is an important touchstone for disclosure, but it's not the only one."

The SEC regularly sends comment letters to companies with questions about disclosures made in securities filings. The letters usually focus on accounting practices, and can result in additional disclosures in future filings, revisions to previously filed disclosures, or a simple response with extra details that help the SEC better understand a disclosure. The back-and-forth between a company and the SEC stops once the regulator deems the matter resolved. The letters are typically made public about 20 days after a matter is resolved.

Sanctions-related letters comprise just a fraction of the SEC's comment letters, but the proportion of companies receiving them grew from 1.5% in 2014 to 4.5% in 2018, according to Audit Analytics. "They're interested in any possible violation of trade restrictions," said Don Whalen, the firm's director of research. "They probably want to make sure sanctions being placed on the countries are actually being honored."

The uptick coincides with "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-sanctions-compliance-fines-hit-decade-high-11564057920", which have been used by the Trump administration to increase pressure on geopolitical issues or target suspected criminal behavior.

In 2018, almost 1,500 individuals or entities were added to the designation list, according to data compiled by Adam M. Smith, a partner at law firm Gibson, Dunn & Crutcher LLP. That is more than twice the annual average over the previous 10 years.

BNY Mellon declined to comment on its SEC correspondence. Ctrip.com didn't respond to a request for comment.

PayPal, the San Jose, Calif.-based financial technology company, received a comment letter in July about its 2018 annual report, in which PayPal disclosed that it self-reported to OFAC suspicious transactions that might have violated U.S. sanctions.

The SEC asked PayPal to detail the countries related to the transactions and the extent of the company's contact with the countries. The regulator also asked PayPal to specify whether those contacts posed a material risk to investors. The correspondence was made public last week.

The SEC declined to comment beyond the letters.

Related Links

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/u-s-sanctions-compliance-fines-hit-decade-high-11564057920"

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/treasury-sets-expectations-for-sanctions-compliance-programs-11556833373"

* "https://www.wsj.com/articles/evolving-venezuela-sanctions-pose-problems-for-banks-11551143320"

The SEC said in its letters to PayPal that many investors, particularly institutional investors, have proposed divestments from companies that do business with U.S.-designated state sponsors of terrorism, and that PayPal should address the potential impact on the investor sentiment, including reputational harm.

Aaron Anderson, PayPal's chief accounting officer, responded to the SEC, writing that the transactions in question, which occurred between 2013 and 2016, were related to North Korea, Iran, Sudan and Syria countries that were under U.S. sanctions at the time of processing. He said in the letter that the revenue from the transactions and their volume were negligible to the company and that OFAC hasn't made a decision about whether it would treat the flagged transactions as potential violations, according to the response sent to the SEC by the company.

A Treasury Department spokeswoman declined to comment, saying the department doesn't speculate on possible sanctions violations or comment on possible or pending investigations.

"PayPal makes every effort to comply with laws and regulations in the markets where we do business around the world," a company spokesman said in an email for this article. "As exhibited by the company's track record of self-reporting and work to satisfy clarifying questions posed by the SEC, PayPal takes its regulatory and compliance obligations seriously."

The regulator and the company had a similar exchange in 2016 related to disclosures in PayPal's 2015 annual report, in which the company said it "https://www.wsj.com/articles/paypal-to-pay-7-7-million-to-u-s-over-alleged-sanctions-violations-1427312161" to OFAC in March 2015 to settle allegations that it processed transactions worth about $44,000 between 2009 and 2013 and had continued to cooperate with OFAC on additional self-reported transactions.

The SEC is looking closely at potential security risks, particularly those related to money-transfer and peer-to-peer payment businesses, said Moshe Katri, an analyst at Wedbush Securities Inc. who follows PayPal.

"These are risks that come when you're processing billions of transactions," Mr. Katri said. "This is going to be a consistent pain point for all of these companies."

WSJ Pro
Canaan Partners Incubates, Leads $10 Million Seed Round for Fintech Startup Bond; The startup, led by former Twilio executive Roy Ng, will offer software to better integrate fintech providers with regional banks

By Yuliya Chernova 
755 words
27 August 2019
12:02
WSJ Pro Venture Capital
RSTPROVC
English
Copyright  2019, Dow Jones & Company, Inc. 

Canaan Partners has led a $10 million seed investment in Bond Financial Technologies Inc., a prelaunch startup that the venture-capital firm began incubating earlier this year.

Bond is setting up a marketplace where companies seeking to offer new loans, debit cards and other financial products can find banks to act as official sponsors. It also has developed software that banks can use to manage and more easily access the data of their fintech clients.

It is rare for Canaan's technology-investment practice to incubate startups, according to Michael Gilroy, a partner at the firm who came up with the idea for Bond.

"For this stage it's absolutely a massive swing for us," Mr. Gilroy said.

Hedge-fund manager Coatue Management also invested in Bond, Mr. Gilroy said. Canaan had planned to write the full check but made room in the round for Coatue, he said. Coatue has been investing in late-stage private companies for years and recently set up a $700 million early-stage practice, The Information reported.

Mr. Gilroy recruited Roy Ng, previously president and chief operating officer of Mapbox Inc. and COO of Twilio Inc., as Bond's chief executive and co-founder. Bond's other co-founders are Matt Bradley, formerly vice president of machine learning at Synapse Financial Technologies Inc., and Yan Wu, who was head of data at Social Finance Inc., or SoFi.

Regulated regional banks in the U.S. have served as the backbone for many new fintech products, beginning with the wave of online lending a decade ago. But such partnerships have been cumbersome, Mr. Gilroy said.

In order for a startup to start offering deposit accounts, debit cards, loans and other financial products, it must work with a regulated bank.

For example, Bancorp Bank of Wilmington, Del., issues debit cards on behalf of mobile banking startup Chime, while Utah-based Celtic Bank issues loans for online lender Kabbage Inc.

Companies would use Bond's platform to send data about their fintech products and customers to partner banks. Banks would use Bond and the data it collects to qualify and sign up new fintech clients and to manage these relationships. Banks also could use Bond to pull information about its fintech clients for regulatory audits. Fintech companies, meanwhile, could use Bond to compare partner banks.

The need for better integration with banks is growing as companies in more industries, from ride-hailing to e-commerce and business software, seek to offer financial products or expand their suite of fintech services, Mr. Gilroy said.

Bond's services would compete to some degree with other venture-backed companies such as Cross River Bank, an FDIC-insured bank that focuses on working with fintech companies, and Synapse, which helps fintech companies launch various banking products.

Current solutions are lacking, Mr. Gilroy said, as another Canaan portfolio company found out. Accounting software startup ScaleFactor Inc., for example, has been trying to start lending to its customers, Mr. Gilroy said, because the company believes it can offer better loans based on the information it has about its customers. It has been working on a bank integration with Salt Lake City-based Celtic Bank.

"We went out and started engaging these banks 18 months ago, and we still don't have a product in the market yet," Mr. Gilroy said of ScaleFactor.

Bond is operating out of Canaan's San Francisco offices but is looking to move out shortly, Mr. Ng said. The company expects to have between 25 and 35 employees by the end of the year.

Mr. Ng said he hopes to have several banks on Bond's platform within a year. "I want to build a brand out there that not only banks and fintechs trust but also the regulators," he said.

Benjamin Lawsky, a former financial services superintendent for New York, is an adviser to Bond and has equity in the startup, Mr. Gilroy said.

The seed deal was the culmination of a long process, Mr. Gilroy said. "It's a lot more work than having a team come in and pitch you," Mr. Gilroy said about incubating a startup. On the upside, the firm holds a bigger stake in Bond than it would receive in a typical seed deal, he said.

WSJ Pro
Reporter's Notebook: Financial-Services Executives Offer AI Lessons; Takeaways from this week's Ai4 Finance conference in New York

By Jared Council 
541 words
23 August 2019
10:30
WSJ Pro Artificial Intelligence
RSTPROAI
English
Copyright  2019 Dow Jones & Company, Inc. All Rights Reserved 

Financial-services companies are embracing artificial intelligence, which International Data Corp. has called a game-changer for the industry.

However, finance executives face challenges in deploying the technology, including potential government regulation, being able to explain how the technology makes a decision, and gaining an AI edge against competitors.

Financial-services executives, data scientists, vendors and others gathered in Midtown Manhattan this week for the Ai4 Finance conference to hear about the latest use cases and lessons from firms that have implemented AI systems.

Among the key takeaways:

Watch for coming regulations. Wendy Callaghan, chief innovation legal officer and associate general counsel at American International Group Inc., said regulatory proposals and guidelines are emerging around AI that companies need to watch carefully. One piece of proposed legislation, the Algorithmic Accountability Act, calls for companies to conduct "impact assessments of highly sensitive automated decision systems." Ms. Callaghan said it's important for companies that might be affected by the law "start having the conversations around the internal governance that they'll need to provide explanations to regulators...that will then pave the way to be prepared for formal impact assessments, if that comes into play." She said another issue regulators are looking at is "human centricity," or keeping humans involved in some aspect of AI decision-making.

Deploy explainability tools. Chris Kovel, Morgan Stanley's head of wealth management analytics, intelligence and data technology, talked about the need for financial firms to be able to understand the factors driving algorithmic decisions, known as explainability. He said the company is using an open-source analysis tool called LIME to explain the recommendations made by an AI system dubbed Next Best Action, which guides its financial advisers on client engagement. Mr. Kovel said he's watching other explainability tools in the market. "[We've] got our eye on future models that we believe will have much more explainability." He also said he disagrees with the notion that AI will never be fully explainable. "Those aren't good answers for regulators. Those aren't good answers for our [financial advisers]. Those aren't good answers for our clients," he said.

Keep innovating. Richard Mathieson, a managing director at BlackRock Inc., which manages $6.84 trillion in total assets, said the company's quantitative trading unit gathers reams of data and analyzes them with natural language processing and machine learning to make investment decisions. He said the unit has learned a number of lessons since hiring its first data scientist roughly a decade ago. One is that innovation must be ongoing, and companies shouldn't rest on their laurels. "We place a huge amount of emphasis on research and innovation at the very, very heart of our investment process," he said. Still, no matter how much a company relies on technology, human expertise remains key, he added. Big data and machine learning are tools that can help answer investment questions, but human expertise and asking the right questions remains critical to developing new investment tools and assessing the results they produce.

Write to Jared Council at "mailto:jared.council@wsj.com"

HANNAH KUCHLER

Technology

Color, a Silicon Valley health technology start-up whose backers include the venture capital company General Catalyst and Laurene Powell Jobs' Emerson Collective, has been hired to provide "genetic counselling" to potentially hundreds of thousands of Americans taking part in a US government research project.

The "All of Us" programme, launched last year by the National Institutes of Health, aims to collect data from 1m participants, looking for patterns in lifestyle, environment and biology that could explain the causes of disease and suggest new treatments.

Unlike some other big data programmes in healthcare, such as the UK Biobank, All of Us will enable people who provide their genetic data to learn what the researchers have discovered about their health.

The contract, worth up to $25m, allows Color to expand its remote counselling service to help participants and their families understand the secrets hidden in their genetic data and the healthcare options they have to tackle any increased likelihood of serious diseases.

Brad Ozenberger, the genomics programme director for the All of Us programme, said returning information to participants was one of its "core values".

"We've heard time and again that people are eager to get information back and learn more about themselves," he said. "We want to do right by all of our participants to get them the information they need and referrals for follow-up care, if appropriate." Investor interest in genetics start-ups has been piqued by the soaring sales of consumer genetic tests available on sites such as 23andMe and Ancestry, unearthing risks of serious disease or family secrets.

Color sells genetic testing packages including support for physicians and counselling to health systems, employers and research institutions. It was founded in 2013 by former Twitter executives Othman Laraki and Elad Gil, former cyber security engineer Nish Bhat and physician Taylor Sittler.

Mr Laraki, chief executive, said it was important to remember that genetic data held sensitive health information. He said the way the data had been used by "genetics hobbyists" had meant there were often not safeguards, including making counselling available so people could understand their results.

Color's five-year contract with the NIH begins with funding of $4.6m for the first year.

Since it opened in May 2018, All of Us has enrolled about 175,000 participants at a network of more than 340 recruitment sites across the US.

The programme, part of a broader government initiative in so-called precision health, aims to harvest data that includes information on 59 genes tied to risk of specific diseases for which there are established medical guidelines for treatment or prevention. Last week, the programme announced that more than 80 per cent of its participants so far were from groups that historically have been under-represented in biomedical research. The programme is working to connect them with care.
